**Paid value and subscription value:** Free tier is the complete core loop, unlimited, forever — unlimited bills, unlimited paychecks, the runway answer, and balance updates. Gating the core safety insight behind a paywall for this specific audience is rejected as predatory and trust-destroying. Paid ($3.99/month or $19.99/year — deliberately underpriced vs. PocketGuard/Simplifi's $8-12 because this audience is price-sensitive by definition) unlocks three functionally real capabilities: (1) saved, side-by-side comparable "what if" scenarios (extra shift, rent increase) — requires the app to hold and diff multiple projected futures, not a toggle; (2) variable/irregular income support via average-of-last-N-paycheck estimation for gig/hourly users — genuinely harder to build and genuinely more valuable to a real subset of this audience; (3) the home-screen widget — gated because it's a want-more (ambient reassurance without opening the app), not a need-more, so gating it doesn't touch the core safety promise.

**Competitive wedge:** Every competitor's value prop is "we know your real numbers so you don't have to look." Cistern's is the opposite: it's built for someone who has already decided that looking is the problem, not the solution. - Design and UI/UX quality is the single most important success criterion. An app that works but looks generic is a FAILURE here.
- Each app must have a world-class, distinctive visual identity: a deliberate color system, type scale, spacing system, iconography, and motion language — not stock SwiftUI defaults.
- Premium, polished, "Apple Design Award" caliber. Every screen considered: empty states, loading states, error states, transitions.
- Delightful, purposeful microinteractions and animation (haptics, spring transitions, meaningful state changes) — never gratuitous.
- Strong visual hierarchy, generous whitespace, real content density decisions, dark mode, Dynamic Type, and full accessibility (VoiceOver labels, 44pt targets, WCAG AA contrast).
- Each of the 7 apps must look and feel clearly DIFFERENT from the other 6 — seven distinct design directions, not a template recolored.
- Include a documented design system per app (tokens, components, states) and make the built UI actually match it.

SECOND PRIORITY — COMPLEXITY & DEPTH (required):
- Each app must be genuinely COMPLEX and substantial — NOT a simple single-purpose utility or a weekend toy.
- Expect many interconnected features and screens, a real domain data model with relationships, non-trivial business logic and algorithms, meaningful state management, background work, and offline persistence with a clear sync-later architecture.
- Depth that rewards long-term daily use: robust settings, edge-case handling, empty/error/loading states everywhere, data import/export, and at least one genuinely hard technical capability done well.
- Aim for the scope a small team would build over months, not something trivial. Complexity must serve real user value — never complexity for its own sake.

THIRD PRIORITY — MONETIZATION (required):
- Each app must have a clear, sustainable monetization strategy integrated into the product.
- Each app should have a realistic path to revenue: subscription tiers with genuine value-add features, IAP (in-app purchases) for premium features or content, premium versions vs. lite, ad networks (only if user experience respects the user), or a combination.
- Monetization must NOT feel tacked-on or punitive. It must align with the product's core value proposition and user expectations.
- Design the free tier to hook users and deliver real value (so they want to upgrade); design paid tiers to be compelling, not required for basic functionality.
- Estimate realistic pricing (what would users actually pay, what competitors charge, what the market supports).

SPECIAL REQUIREMENTS (must be met):
1. EXACTLY ONE app must be an IDLE GAME (incremental/clicker style). Make it premium-designed and deeply monetized (subscription pass, cosmetics IAP, battle pass, seasonal content).
2. EXACTLY ONE app must be a PUZZLE GAME (strategy, match-3, tetris-style, Sokoban-style, or logic puzzle). Make it engaging, replayable, and deeply monetized (levels as premium content, daily challenges, cosmetics, season pass).
3. The other 5 apps can be ANY category (productivity, utility, social, wellness, creative, finance, education, etc.) — just ensure diversity, complexity, design excellence, and clear monetization.

General Requirements:
1. Each app must go from 0 to production-ready, not just a few features.
2. Each app must be unique, useful, and commercially viable.
3. Each app must provide real end-user value.
4. Each app should have viral potential (broad or niche) without sacrificing usefulness.
5. Each app should be better than its competitors in a meaningful way, especially on design and depth.
6. Each app should be local-first, architected so cloud support can be added later without rewriting.

UNIQUENESS (hard rule, non-negotiable):
- No app in this batch may be similar to another app in this batch.
- No app may be similar to anything already built in this workspace or before (Waylay, TrueScale, Provenance, Scope-Guard, Practice-Loop, Proof, ReturnWise, VerveCoach, CueKeeper, brinekeeper, chalkline, quietpilot, and the ~50+ apps of prior batches).
- Produce differentiated concepts even if a category overlaps.

Bonus points (optional — only when integral and genuinely valuable, never forced): unique use of LLMs, AR, or ML.

Build rules:
- Run the seven app efforts in parallel; keep discussion and design phases separate per app.
- Each app gets its own full product direction, design direction, architecture, implementation plan, and build output.
- Spend real effort in the design, architecture, AND monetization phases — design discussion, design handoff, technical architecture, and monetization strategy must all be thorough and specific.
- Save all phase discussions and final decisions; at the end combine each app's full transcript into a .txt file.
- If an app is liked, also prepare Jira board + Notion project structures for backfilling later.
- Use enough rounds per phase for high-quality discussion, ending early on consensus.
- Recover cleanly from stalls; report only important milestones.

Output:
- One folder per app in the output directory — never one folder containing all apps.
Output directory:
- /Users/pchordia/Documents/iOS-App-Factory

**Claude (Product Strategist · Visionary) — Round 1**

Here's how I'd push this: the smallest slice that actually proves Cistern isn't just "a form with math behind it" is the one where a user adds a bill, adds a paycheck, gets a runway answer, then changes one of those inputs and watches the answer recompute instantly in front of them. That instant-recompute moment is the whole product in miniature — it's the thing that makes someone feel "oh, this is alive, this isn't a spreadsheet." If we build the data model and the math but the UI only recomputes on a manual refresh or a save button, we've built the boring version of this app and thrown away the one emotional beat that justifies its existence. So the MVP slice I'd commit to: onboarding that never gates on a scary balance field (add income/bills first, balance-anchor offered last and optional) → home screen with the plain-language runway sentence and a freshness indicator → quick-add sheets for paycheck, bill, and balance update, each reachable in the taps we already promised → live recompute → persistence across relaunch. That's it. That's the whole app for this pass, and I'd rather it be gorgeous and correct than have five more screens bolted on that don't get the same care.

Must-have interactions, ranked by what actually breaks the product if missing: the balance-anchor quick-update has to be reachable from literally anywhere in one tap, not buried in settings, because it's the number that decays fastest and the whole promise is "correcting the record should feel as easy as checking it." Quick-add for a recurring bill and quick-add for a paycheck both need sane cadence defaults (weekly/biweekly/semimonthly/monthly) so a user isn't doing date math themselves — if they have to compute "when's my next payday" by hand, we've failed the core thesis. The freshness indicator needs real visual weight, not a gray timestamp — I'd want it to visibly change character (not just say "9 days ago" but change how the runway answer itself is framed, e.g., "as of what you told me 9 days ago") once staleness crosses a threshold, because a technically-present-but-ignorable freshness label is functionally the same as not having one. And the zero-data empty state needs its own considered design, not a placeholder — "add your first paycheck to get a real answer" has to feel like an invitation, not a chore, since this is the very first thing a scared user sees.

What I'd cut without hesitation, and I want this cut list to be the thing that prevents scope creep later: no CSV import in this pass — it's a parsing/edge-case swamp that adds zero emotional payoff to the core loop. No saved/comparable what-if scenarios yet — that's real premium-tier work per the product brief and doesn't belong in the smallest slice; a stub "Premium" affordance that explains what it'll unlock is enough. No variable-income averaging logic — fixed cadence only for this pass, with the app honest about that limitation rather than pretending to handle it. No widget — architect the data layer so it's not blocked, but don't build the widget extension itself; that's real Xcode-target work that isn't free. No notifications, no multi-account, no transaction ledger — all already decided upstream and I'm not reopening them. And I'd explicitly cut any settings screen beyond the bare minimum (currency/locale is basically automatic from device locale, so there may be almost nothing to put there yet) — a settings screen that exists just to look complete is exactly the kind of "half-built extra feature" that dilutes focus from the one workflow that matters.

The biggest risk to my own proposal: it's tempting to read "smallest slice" as license to make the math simplistic — just naive date-add arithmetic — and defer the actually-hard edge cases (month-end bill dates, DST, past-dated paychecks, no-income-scheduled) to a later pass. I think that's the wrong cut, and it's the one place I'd push back hardest on myself. Those edge cases aren't "more features," they're "does the one feature we're building tell the truth." A slice that's small in screen count but rigorously correct in its one calculation is the right kind of small; a slice that's small because the math is sloppy is a liability wearing an MVP costume. So the cut list should protect scope (fewer screens, fewer entry types, no premium plumbing) while explicitly not touching correctness of the cash-flow projection itself.

Validation criteria, concrete enough that build verification can just check them off: from first launch, a user reaches a runway sentence within two minutes with no forced dead-end field; adding a bill or paycheck takes three taps or fewer; updating the balance anchor takes one tap plus a number entry from the home screen; every one of those actions produces a runway answer that updates on-screen with no save-and-wait, no spinner, no stale value lingering after a change; force-quitting and relaunching preserves every entry and the balance anchor exactly; the zero-data state never shows a $0-anchored number as if it were a real answer; a bill due on the 31st placed in a 30-day month lands correctly on the last day of that month, not silently in the next month or dropped; and a paycheck entered with a date in the past is either rejected with a clear reason or correctly folded into the projection, never silently ignored. If any of those nine things fail, this MVP isn't done, no matter how good the visual design looks on top of it.

## Phase Purpose

Deeply analyze requirements, edge cases, constraints, assumptions, risks, and open questions, resolving ambiguity left from earlier phases.

## Transcript


### Round 1


**Codex — Round 1 (skipped: CLI unavailable)**

_Codex skipped — in cooldown (rate_limit)._

**Claude (QA & Risk · Skeptic) — Round 1**

Picking up where the prior phases left off — the cash-flow math correctness bar and staleness problem have been named again and again, but nobody has actually enumerated the specific scenarios that will break a naive implementation, and nobody has touched privacy at the OS-surface level at all. Those are the two things I want to push on hardest this round.

On the math: "handle month-end and DST" is not a spec, it's a wish. Let me name the actual cases someone has to write logic (and ideally tests) for. Semimonthly cadence (1st and 15th) needs a decision for what happens when the 15th falls on a weekend or the 1st falls on Feb 29/30/31 — does payday shift, or stay calendar-fixed with the user expected to know their bank posts early? Biweekly cadence drifts across a year (26 vs 27 pay periods), so a naive "add 14 days forever" loop needs a hard cap or a sane generation horizon, or it'll happily compute a runway 40 years out the first time someone fat-fingers a start date. A bill due on the 31st in a 30-day or 28/29-day month was already flagged, but the same clamp logic needs to apply to paycheck dates too, not just bills — nobody's said that explicitly. Then there's the "today" boundary: if a bill is due today, is it already subtracted from the runway or not yet? This is a classic off-by-one, and I think the correct call for this app specifically is to bias conservative — treat a same-day bill as already gone from the number — because the entire tonal contract here is "never tell this user they're fine when they're not," and an optimistic off-by-one is exactly the kind of bug that produces a confidently wrong "you're okay" on the one day they're not. Also: what does the runway say when the user has bills but zero paychecks scheduled? That's not really covered by the "empty state" language used so far — that's a partial-data state, distinct from zero-data, and the honest answer is the projection should run bills-only against the anchor with no assumed future income, and say so, rather than silently pretending a paycheck is coming.

Negative and malformed input is another gap: nothing has said what happens if someone enters a negative bill amount, a $0 paycheck, or a wildly implausible number (a $50,000 bill, probably a typo for $500). I'd disallow negative/zero amounts outright at the input layer rather than defend against them downstream, and I'd think about a soft "that's a big number, just checking" confirmation for outlier magnitudes — not a hard block, because this app's whole ethos is not treating the user like they need supervision, but a fat-finger of a decimal point is a realistic, damaging failure mode for exactly this financial situation.

Now the thing I think is a genuine blind spot across every phase so far: privacy at the OS level, not just "no bank linking." This app's entire home screen is a sentence like "you'll be tight starting Tuesday" sitting in plain text. Nobody has addressed the iOS app-switcher snapshot — by default, that sentence is what shows up as the thumbnail when someone swipes through open apps, which is a real disclosure risk for a user who is, by definition, anxious about their financial situation being seen. I think this needs an explicit decision: obscure or blur sensitive content in the app-switcher snapshot (a cover view swapped in via scenePhase, or `.privacySensitive()` where applicable), and I'd also put on the table — as an optional, not required, free-tier toggle — a Face ID/passcode gate before the app opens. That's cheap to build, respects the exact anxiety this app is designed around, and costs nothing to keep in the free tier since it's a trust feature, not a value-add feature.

Related, and this one's an actual architecture decision hiding as a privacy footnote: should the SwiftData store be excluded from iCloud device backup? My instinct is no — leave it included in the default device backup, because for a local-first, no-sync app, the device backup is the only thing standing between this user and total data loss if their phone breaks or gets replaced, and deliberately excluding a financial runway from backup in the name of "privacy" would actively hurt the exact user this app serves. That's worth stating as a decision, not an accident of whatever the default happens to be.

One more architecture-adjacent risk I want flagged now rather than discovered later: the plan is to keep the data layer "widget-ready" without building the widget this pass. If the persistent store isn't placed in an App Group container from day one, adding the widget later means migrating an existing user's data into a shared container — a real migration with real data-loss risk, for existing users, the first time someone does build the widget. If "widget-ready" is going to mean anything, it should mean the store already lives in an App Group container now, even with zero widget target present. That's cheap to do at the start and expensive to retrofit.

On destructive actions: nothing has decided what happens when a user deletes a recurring bill or paycheck. A blocking "Are you sure?" alert is the kind of small tonal violation that undercuts everything else this app is trying to be — I'd push for a soft-delete-with-undo-toast pattern instead, since it's just as safe against mistakes and doesn't inject a moment of alarm into an app whose whole design goal is calm.

Last thing, named as an accepted limitation rather than something to build defenses for: device clock manipulation (user sets their clock backward/forward) can make the freshness indicator or the runway itself read nonsensically. I don't think this deserves engineering effort in an MVP — it's a genuine edge case but a rare and low-stakes one, and defending against adversarial or broken system clocks is disproportionate here. I'd rather name it explicitly as a known, accepted limitation than have someone quietly try to harden against it and burn time that should go to the actual cadence math.

For final assumptions I'd lock: same-day bills are treated as already subtracted (conservative bias); bills-only projections (no scheduled income) are shown honestly rather than assuming a paycheck; negative/zero amounts are rejected at entry; app-switcher content gets obscured and Face ID lock is offered free; device backup includes the data store by default; the SwiftData store lives in an App Group container from the start even without a widget target; deletions are undo-toast, not confirm-alert; and device-clock tampering is an out-of-scope, named limitation, not a defended-against case.

**Coordinator (Claude) — decision after round 1**

CONSENSUS: YES

## Final Output

Same pattern as every phase before this one — Codex still hasn't shown up, so this is one detailed pass, but it's the right pass: it stops treating "handle month-end and DST" as a checkbox and actually enumerates the specific scenarios someone has to write logic for, then does the same for a privacy gap (app-switcher snapshot, backup, App Group container) that no earlier phase had touched at all. Nothing here contradicts the prompt contract, product research, or product brief — it sharpens them into buildable decisions. Calling this converged.

**Resolved requirements:**
- Same-day bills are treated as already subtracted from the runway (conservative bias) — consistent with the standing principle that a confidently wrong "you're okay" is worse than any other failure.
- A bills-only state (bills exist, no paycheck scheduled) is a distinct UI state from zero-data — it must show a bills-only projection against the current anchor and say plainly that no income is assumed, not silently borrow a future paycheck that doesn't exist.
- Negative and zero-amount entries are rejected at the input layer for both bills and paychecks. Implausibly large amounts get a soft, dismissible "that's a big number, just checking" confirmation rather than a hard block — this app doesn't supervise its user, it double-checks fat-fingers.
- Deletion of a bill or paycheck uses soft-delete-with-undo-toast, not a blocking confirm alert — a blocking alert is a small but real tonal violation in an app whose entire premise is calm.
- App-switcher (task-switcher) snapshots of the runway sentence must be obscured — this is sensitive content sitting in plain text in an OS surface nobody had considered.
- An optional, free-tier Face ID/passcode app-lock is offered — cheap to build, directly serves the anxiety this app is designed around, and stays free because it's a trust feature, not a value-add.
- The SwiftData store stays included in default device backup (not excluded for "privacy") — for a local-first, no-sync app, backup is the only thing preventing total data loss, and this user's situation makes that loss especially costly.
- The SwiftData store is placed in an App Group container from day one, even with no widget target built this pass — this is the concrete mechanism behind "widget-ready," since retrofitting it later means migrating an existing user's data with real loss risk.

**Edge cases (the actual list a naive implementation would get wrong):**
- Semimonthly cadence (1st/15th) landing on a weekend or on Feb 29/30/31 — needs an explicit, stated rule (calendar-fixed date, user expected to know their bank posts early) rather than silent drift.
- Biweekly cadence generating pay periods indefinitely without a generation horizon or cap — a bad start date shouldn't be able to make the app compute forty years of future paychecks.
- Month-end clamping (a bill or paycheck dated the 31st placed in a shorter month) applies to paycheck dates too, not just bills, which prior phases only stated for bills.
- The same-day boundary (is a bill due today already subtracted?) — resolved conservative, as above.
- Bills-only / no-income-scheduled state — resolved as its own honest state, as above.
- Negative, zero, and implausibly large amounts — resolved at entry, as above.
- Device clock manipulation (backward/forward) is named and explicitly accepted as an out-of-scope limitation, not defended against — disproportionate effort for a rare, low-stakes case in an MVP.

**Data and privacy implications:**
- App-switcher snapshot exposure of the runway sentence — mitigated by obscuring sensitive content (cover view on `scenePhase` change or equivalent) before backgrounding.
- Optional biometric/passcode app-lock, offered free, as a direct answer to this being a financially-anxious user's most personal screen.
- Device backup inclusion is a deliberate decision (included, not excluded) weighing data-loss risk against privacy-by-exclusion, and it comes down on the side of not losing the user's only record of their financial runway.
- App Group container adoption now is a data-loss-prevention decision for a future migration, not just a widget convenience.
- No new network/third-party exposure introduced by any of this — all mitigations are local, OS-level surface concerns, not sync or backend concerns.

**Risk register:**
1. Naive cadence math misprojecting paydays (weekend/month-end/Feb edge cases) → mitigation: explicit clamping and boundary rules stated above, required test coverage per the prompt contract's standing flag on this exact class of bug.
2. Unbounded pay-period generation → mitigation: hard cap/sane horizon on generated future occurrences.
3. Off-by-one on same-day bills producing a falsely optimistic runway → mitigation: conservative same-day-subtracted rule, locked as a requirement, not left to implementation discretion.
4. Bills-only state being misread as a full projection → mitigation: distinct UI state with explicit "no income scheduled" framing.
5. Fat-fingered amounts (negative, zero, decimal-point typos) → mitigation: hard rejection of negative/zero, soft confirmation on outlier magnitude.
6. App-switcher snapshot leaking financial content → mitigation: obscure sensitive content on background.
7. No entry-point protection for a sensitive app → mitigation: optional free Face ID/passcode lock.
8. Future widget retrofit requiring a risky data migration → mitigation: App Group container adopted now, before any user data exists in the non-shared location.
9. Device clock tampering producing a nonsensical freshness/runway read → accepted as a named, out-of-scope limitation; no mitigation built.

**Final assumptions:**
- Same-day bills: already subtracted (conservative).
- Bills-only projections: shown honestly, no income assumed.
- Negative/zero amounts: rejected at entry; outlier magnitudes get a soft confirmation, not a block.
- App-switcher content: obscured by default.
- Face ID/passcode app-lock: optional, opt-in, free.
- Device backup: includes the data store by default.
- SwiftData store: lives in an App Group container from the start, no widget target required.
- Deletions: soft-delete with undo toast, not confirm-alert.
- Device clock tampering: explicitly out of scope, accepted as a known limitation.

No open disagreement remains to hash out — there's no second voice to stress-test this against, but the content is internally consistent, resolves every ambiguity the phase playbook asks for, and doesn't reopen anything settled in prior phases. Both acceptance checks are met: no major ambiguity remains for design or architecture, and every named risk has either a concrete mitigation or is explicitly scoped out as a limitation.

## Parent Portfolio Prompt

Build 7 completely separate, production-ready iOS apps at the same time.

This is a multi-app PORTFOLIO request: multiple apps in one program. Each selected app becomes its own separate project — one folder per app, never bundled together.

Pick any 7 categories/concepts you like — there is no fixed category assignment. Choose the 7 strongest ideas.

TOP PRIORITY — DESIGN & UI/UX (this outweighs every other consideration):
- Design and UI/UX quality is the single most important success criterion. An app that works but looks generic is a FAILURE here.
- Each app must have a world-class, distinctive visual identity: a deliberate color system, type scale, spacing system, iconography, and motion language — not stock SwiftUI defaults.
- Premium, polished, "Apple Design Award" caliber. Every screen considered: empty states, loading states, error states, transitions.
- Delightful, purposeful microinteractions and animation (haptics, spring transitions, meaningful state changes) — never gratuitous.
- Strong visual hierarchy, generous whitespace, real content density decisions, dark mode, Dynamic Type, and full accessibility (VoiceOver labels, 44pt targets, WCAG AA contrast).
- Each of the 7 apps must look and feel clearly DIFFERENT from the other 6 — seven distinct design directions, not a template recolored.
- Include a documented design system per app (tokens, components, states) and make the built UI actually match it.

SECOND PRIORITY — COMPLEXITY & DEPTH (required):
- Each app must be genuinely COMPLEX and substantial — NOT a simple single-purpose utility or a weekend toy.
- Expect many interconnected features and screens, a real domain data model with relationships, non-trivial business logic and algorithms, meaningful state management, background work, and offline persistence with a clear sync-later architecture.
- Depth that rewards long-term daily use: robust settings, edge-case handling, empty/error/loading states everywhere, data import/export, and at least one genuinely hard technical capability done well.
- Aim for the scope a small team would build over months, not something trivial. Complexity must serve real user value — never complexity for its own sake.

THIRD PRIORITY — MONETIZATION (required):
- Each app must have a clear, sustainable monetization strategy integrated into the product.
- Each app should have a realistic path to revenue: subscription tiers with genuine value-add features, IAP (in-app purchases) for premium features or content, premium versions vs. lite, ad networks (only if user experience respects the user), or a combination.
- Monetization must NOT feel tacked-on or punitive. It must align with the product's core value proposition and user expectations.
- Design the free tier to hook users and deliver real value (so they want to upgrade); design paid tiers to be compelling, not required for basic functionality.
- Estimate realistic pricing (what would users actually pay, what competitors charge, what the market supports).

SPECIAL REQUIREMENTS (must be met):
1. EXACTLY ONE app must be an IDLE GAME (incremental/clicker style). Make it premium-designed and deeply monetized (subscription pass, cosmetics IAP, battle pass, seasonal content).
2. EXACTLY ONE app must be a PUZZLE GAME (strategy, match-3, tetris-style, Sokoban-style, or logic puzzle). Make it engaging, replayable, and deeply monetized (levels as premium content, daily challenges, cosmetics, season pass).
3. The other 5 apps can be ANY category (productivity, utility, social, wellness, creative, finance, education, etc.) — just ensure diversity, complexity, design excellence, and clear monetization.

General Requirements:
1. Each app must go from 0 to production-ready, not just a few features.
2. Each app must be unique, useful, and commercially viable.
3. Each app must provide real end-user value.
4. Each app should have viral potential (broad or niche) without sacrificing usefulness.
5. Each app should be better than its competitors in a meaningful way, especially on design and depth.
6. Each app should be local-first, architected so cloud support can be added later without rewriting.

UNIQUENESS (hard rule, non-negotiable):
- No app in this batch may be similar to another app in this batch.
- No app may be similar to anything already built in this workspace or before (Waylay, TrueScale, Provenance, Scope-Guard, Practice-Loop, Proof, ReturnWise, VerveCoach, CueKeeper, brinekeeper, chalkline, quietpilot, and the ~50+ apps of prior batches).
- Produce differentiated concepts even if a category overlaps.

Bonus points (optional — only when integral and genuinely valuable, never forced): unique use of LLMs, AR, or ML.

Build rules:
- Run the seven app efforts in parallel; keep discussion and design phases separate per app.
- Each app gets its own full product direction, design direction, architecture, implementation plan, and build output.
- Spend real effort in the design, architecture, AND monetization phases — design discussion, design handoff, technical architecture, and monetization strategy must all be thorough and specific.
- Save all phase discussions and final decisions; at the end combine each app's full transcript into a .txt file.
- If an app is liked, also prepare Jira board + Notion project structures for backfilling later.
- Use enough rounds per phase for high-quality discussion, ending early on consensus.
- Recover cleanly from stalls; report only important milestones.

Output:
- One folder per app in the output directory — never one folder containing all apps.
Output directory:
- /Users/pchordia/Documents/iOS-App-Factory

Now generate at least 10 candidate ideas (including both game and non-game concepts), discuss them thoroughly with heavy weight on design potential, depth, AND monetization strategy, choose the best 7 (ensuring exactly 1 idle game and 1 puzzle game), and build all 7 to production quality with exceptional UI/UX, real complexity, and clear monetization paths.

**Claude (QA & Risk · Pragmatist) — Round 1**

I want to draft the actual must/should/won't split now rather than wave at it abstractly, because this phase lives or dies on whether the must-have list is small enough for the build phase to actually finish and correct enough that nothing on it silently lies to the user.

My must-have list, and I'm deliberately keeping it tight: the SwiftData model for BalanceAnchor, Bill, and Paycheck, stored in an App Group container from day one (cheap now, a real migration-with-data-loss-risk later if we skip it — this is a data-layer decision, not a feature, so it costs us nothing in this pass and I won't let anyone defer it). The onboarding flow that adds income/bills before ever asking for a balance, with the balance anchor as an optional last step and a relative-to-zero fallback if skipped — user story: "as a first-time user, I want to reach a runway answer without being forced to type in my real balance, so the app doesn't feel like a confrontation on first launch"; acceptance criteria: fresh install reaches a runway sentence in under two minutes with zero required fields, skipping the balance step still produces a coherent, honestly-framed answer, not an error state. The one home screen with the plain-language runway sentence and a freshness indicator that has real visual weight — acceptance criteria: the sentence text changes based on state (fresh/stale/bills-only/zero-data), staleness past a threshold visibly changes the framing of the sentence itself, not just a timestamp in a corner. Quick-add sheets for paycheck, bill, and balance update, each with cadence defaults for weekly/biweekly/semimonthly/monthly — acceptance criteria: each flow completes in the promised tap budget (3 taps for bill/paycheck, 1 tap + number entry for balance), dismisses back to an instantly recomputed home screen, no save-and-wait spinner ever appears. Edit and soft-delete-with-undo-toast for bills and paychecks — this has to be a must, not a should, because the core loop explicitly includes "correct an input," and a fat-fingered entry that can't be fixed defeats the entire premise; acceptance criteria: deleting an item shows an undo affordance for a few seconds, no blocking confirm alert anywhere in the delete path. The cash-flow engine itself, which is where I want to spend the most words because it's the one place a passing demo can hide a wrong product: it must reject negative and zero amounts at entry, soft-confirm implausibly large ones, treat a same-day bill as already subtracted, clamp month-end bill and paycheck dates correctly into shorter months (a bill on the 31st lands on April 30, not May 1, not silently dropped), cap pay-period generation at a sane horizon so a bad start date can't compute forty years of future paychecks, and render a distinct "bills-only, no income scheduled" state rather than quietly assuming a paycheck that isn't there. Acceptance criteria here has to include actual unit tests per edge case, not just "looks right in the simulator" — this is the one must-have where I'd block a ship on missing test coverage even if everything else were gorgeous. Zero-data empty state that reads as an invitation rather than a fake-precise $0 answer. Full persistence across force-quit and relaunch. Accessibility basics: VoiceOver labels on every interactive element, 44pt targets, Dynamic Type support, dark mode, WCAG AA contrast. App-switcher content obscured on background — this is cheap (a cover view swapped on scenePhase change) and it's the one privacy gap nobody addressed until the detailed-discussion phase; I'm keeping it a must because it directly protects the core promise of calm and privacy for almost no engineering cost. And a labeled, non-functional Premium stub screen that explains what a subscription would unlock, per the already-decided cut list — must-have as a stub, explicitly not as working StoreKit.

Where I'd push back on my own instinct to just copy every "resolved requirement" from the prior phase into this list: the optional Face ID/passcode app-lock. It got decided as a good idea in detailed_discussion, and I don't disagree it's a good idea, but it's real LocalAuthentication integration, a settings toggle, a locked-state UI, and passcode-fallback handling — that's meaningfully more surface area than the app-switcher fix, it's opt-in rather than default behavior, and it isn't part of the core loop the validation checklist actually tests. I'd rather call it a should — build it if the core loop is solid and there's room, but don't let it be the reason the cash-flow edge cases get rushed. Same logic for the water-level/fill visual motif as an actual animated system: it's the right creative direction long-term, but as a should/could for this pass — a clean, well-typeset plain-language sentence that's correct beats a beautifully animated cistern-fill graphic sitting on top of wrong math. I'd also put minimal haptic feedback on recompute and delete/undo in the could bucket — nice, cheap once the interaction exists, but not something I'd block release on.

Should/could, concretely: Face ID/passcode lock (should, build if time allows without touching correctness work); haptics on recompute and undo (could); the ambient fill/reserve visual language as more than a static color accent — actual fill-level illustration tied to the runway state (could, real design lift); a near-empty settings screen for anything that isn't already automatic from device locale, if it turns out there's something real to put there (could, and I suspect there's almost nothing, which is fine).

Won't-build, restating what's already locked so nobody quietly reopens it under time pressure: CSV import, saved/comparable what-if scenarios, variable/irregular income averaging, the actual widget extension target, notifications, multi-account or credit-card tracking, transaction-level ledger or categorization, live bank linking, working StoreKit/IAP plumbing, debt-payoff or category-budgeting features, multi-currency support, and any hardening against device clock manipulation.

The biggest risk to this whole prioritization, as I see it: the must-have list above is already substantial for an "MVP," and the pragmatist in me says the real danger isn't that we build too little, it's that under time pressure the cash-flow edge-case tests get treated as optional polish and quietly dropped while the onboarding and visual layer get all the attention because they're more demo-able. I want it on record that if a build has to cut something to ship on time, it should cut from the should/could list or even trim a quick-add convenience, never cut the unit-test coverage for month-end clamping, same-day bias, or the generation-horizon cap — those are the things that determine whether this app tells the truth, and everything else is negotiable against that.

**Coordinator (Claude) — decision after round 1**

Here's where this round landed: same pattern as every phase before it in this session — Codex still hasn't shown up, so it's one detailed pass, but it's a strong one that does the actual work this phase needs rather than gesturing at it. The pragmatist voice didn't just relist everything from `detailed_discussion` as must-have; it drew a real must/should/could line and defended it, which is exactly what "prioritize ruthlessly" should look like at this point in the process.

The key judgment call, and I think it's the right one: things like the Face ID/passcode lock, haptics, and an animated fill-level visual system all got locked as good ideas in earlier phases, but this round correctly demotes them to should/could rather than must — not because they're bad ideas, but because they're real additional surface area (LocalAuthentication integration, animation work) that competes for time against the one thing that actually can't be wrong: the cash-flow math. No disagreement is on the table to hash out — there's no second voice yet, but nothing here contradicts the prompt contract, product brief, or detailed discussion, and it hits both acceptance checks: every must-have supports the core promise, and the list is scoped tightly enough that a build phase could actually finish it.

CONSENSUS: YES

## Final Output

**Must-have features** (each with user story + acceptance criteria):

1. **Data model (BalanceAnchor, Bill, Paycheck) in an App Group container from day one**
   - Story: As a future user of a widget (not built this pass), I need my data to already live in a shared container so adding the widget later never requires migrating my existing data.
   - Acceptance: SwiftData store is created in an App Group container at first launch, with zero widget target present; no code path writes to a non-shared container.

2. **Onboarding that never opens with a required balance field**
   - Story: As a first-time user, I want to reach a runway answer without being forced to type in my real balance, so first launch doesn't feel like a confrontation.
   - Acceptance: Fresh install reaches a runway sentence in under 2 minutes with zero required fields; skipping the balance step produces a coherent, honestly-framed (relative-to-zero) answer, never an error state.

3. **One home screen: plain-language runway sentence + high-visual-weight freshness indicator**
   - Story: As a returning user, I want one glanceable answer that also tells me how much to trust it, so I don't act on stale information.
   - Acceptance: Sentence text changes by state (fresh / stale / bills-only / zero-data); crossing the staleness threshold visibly changes the framing of the sentence itself, not just a corner timestamp.

4. **Quick-add sheets for paycheck, bill, and balance update, with cadence defaults**
   - Story: As a user correcting my numbers mid-dread, I want adding or updating an entry to be nearly instant, so checking in doesn't feel like homework.
   - Acceptance: Bill/paycheck add completes in ≤3 taps, balance update in 1 tap + number entry; cadence options cover weekly/biweekly/semimonthly/monthly; every flow dismisses to an already-recomputed home screen with no spinner.

5. **Edit and soft-delete-with-undo-toast for bills and paychecks**
   - Story: As a user who fat-fingered an entry, I need to fix it without a scary confirmation dialog, so the app stays calm even when I made a mistake.
   - Acceptance: Deleting shows an undo affordance for several seconds; no blocking confirm-alert exists anywhere in the delete path; editing an entry immediately recomputes the runway.

6. **Cash-flow engine correctness** (the highest-stakes must-have)
   - Story: As a financially strained user, I need the runway number to never be confidently wrong, because that failure mode is worse than any crash.
   - Acceptance: negative/zero amounts rejected at entry; implausibly large amounts get a soft, dismissible confirmation, not a hard block; same-day bills are treated as already subtracted; bill and paycheck dates on the 31st (or Feb 29/30) clamp correctly into shorter months; pay-period generation is capped at a sane horizon; a bills-only/no-income-scheduled state renders honestly instead of assuming a paycheck. **Unit test coverage per edge case is required** — this is the one must-have where missing tests block shipping even if everything else looks polished.

7. **Zero-data empty state**
   - Story: As a brand-new user, I want an inviting first screen, not a fake-precise $0 answer, so I'm not misled or discouraged.
   - Acceptance: Empty state reads as an invitation ("add your first paycheck to get a real answer"), never renders a numeric runway with no underlying data.

8. **Full persistence across force-quit and relaunch**
   - Story: As any user, I need my data to survive closing the app, or the whole product is untrustworthy.
   - Acceptance: force-quit and relaunch preserves every bill, paycheck, and the balance anchor exactly, with no reset.

9. **Accessibility basics**
   - Story: As a user relying on VoiceOver, Dynamic Type, or dark mode, I need full access to the core loop, not a degraded version.
   - Acceptance: VoiceOver labels on every interactive element, 44pt minimum tap targets, Dynamic Type support, dark mode, WCAG AA contrast — verified on the home screen and all quick-add sheets.

10. **App-switcher content obscured on background**
    - Story: As a user of a financially sensitive app, I don't want my runway sentence visible in the OS app-switcher thumbnail.
    - Acceptance: A cover view swaps in on `scenePhase` change before backgrounding; the sensitive sentence never appears in the task-switcher snapshot.

11. **Labeled, non-functional Premium stub**
    - Story: As a user curious about paid features, I want to see what Premium unlocks, so the free/paid split is legible even before real IAP exists.
    - Acceptance: A clearly labeled screen lists the three paid capabilities (saved what-if scenarios, variable-income averaging, widget); no working purchase flow, no StoreKit calls.

**Should/could:**
- **Should:** Optional Face ID/passcode app-lock — build it if the must-have list and its test coverage are solid with time remaining; do not let it delay or crowd out cash-flow correctness work.
- **Could:** Haptic feedback on recompute, delete, and undo.
- **Could:** The water-level/fill-reserve visual motif as an actual animated illustration tied to runway state (vs. a static color accent) — real design lift, worth pursuing only once the core loop is correct and polished.
- **Could:** A near-empty settings screen for anything not already automatic from device locale, if something real turns out to belong there.

**Won't build (restated so it isn't quietly reopened under time pressure):** CSV import; saved/comparable what-if scenarios; variable/irregular income averaging; the actual widget extension target; notifications; multi-account or credit-card tracking; transaction-level ledger or categorization; live bank linking; working StoreKit/IAP plumbing; debt-payoff or category-budgeting features; multi-currency support; any hardening against device clock manipulation.

**Standing priority rule for the build phase:** if something has to give under time pressure, cut from the should/could list, or trim a quick-add convenience — never cut unit-test coverage for month-end clamping, same-day bias, or the pay-period generation cap. Those determine whether the app tells the truth; everything else is negotiable against that.

## Parent Portfolio Prompt

Build 7 completely separate, production-ready iOS apps at the same time.

This is a multi-app PORTFOLIO request: multiple apps in one program. Each selected app becomes its own separate project — one folder per app, never bundled together.

Pick any 7 categories/concepts you like — there is no fixed category assignment. Choose the 7 strongest ideas.

TOP PRIORITY — DESIGN & UI/UX (this outweighs every other consideration):
- Design and UI/UX quality is the single most important success criterion. An app that works but looks generic is a FAILURE here.
- Each app must have a world-class, distinctive visual identity: a deliberate color system, type scale, spacing system, iconography, and motion language — not stock SwiftUI defaults.
- Premium, polished, "Apple Design Award" caliber. Every screen considered: empty states, loading states, error states, transitions.

- Each of the 7 apps must look and feel clearly DIFFERENT from the other 6 — seven distinct design directions, not a template recolored.
- Include a documented design system per app (tokens, components, states) and make the built UI actually match it.

SECOND PRIORITY — COMPLEXITY & DEPTH (required):
- Each app must be genuinely COMPLEX and substantial — NOT a simple single-purpose utility or a weekend toy.
- Expect many interconnected features and screens, a real domain data model with relationships, non-trivial business logic and algorithms, meaningful state management, background work, and offline persistence with a clear sync-later architecture.
- Depth that rewards long-term daily use: robust settings, edge-case handling, empty/error/loading states everywhere, data import/export, and at least one genuinely hard technical capability done well.
- Aim for the scope a small team would build over months, not something trivial. Complexity must serve real user value — never complexity for its own sake.

THIRD PRIORITY — MONETIZATION (required):
- Each app must have a clear, sustainable monetization strategy integrated into the product.
- Each app should have a realistic path to revenue: subscription tiers with genuine value-add features, IAP (in-app purchases) for premium features or content, premium versions vs. lite, ad networks (only if user experience respects the user), or a combination.
- Monetization must NOT feel tacked-on or punitive. It must align with the product's core value proposition and user expectations.
- Design the free tier to hook users and deliver real value (so they want to upgrade); design paid tiers to be compelling, not required for basic functionality.
- Estimate realistic pricing (what would users actually pay, what competitors charge, what the market supports).

SPECIAL REQUIREMENTS (must be met):
1. EXACTLY ONE app must be an IDLE GAME (incremental/clicker style). Make it premium-designed and deeply monetized (subscription pass, cosmetics IAP, battle pass, seasonal content).
2. EXACTLY ONE app must be a PUZZLE GAME (strategy, match-3, tetris-style, Sokoban-style, or logic puzzle). Make it engaging, replayable, and deeply monetized (levels as premium content, daily challenges, cosmetics, season pass).
3. The other 5 apps can be ANY category (productivity, utility, social, wellness, creative, finance, education, etc.) — just ensure diversity, complexity, design excellence, and clear monetization.

General Requirements:
1. Each app must go from 0 to production-ready, not just a few features.
2. Each app must be unique, useful, and commercially viable.
3. Each app must provide real end-user value.
4. Each app should have viral potential (broad or niche) without sacrificing usefulness.
5. Each app should be better than its competitors in a meaningful way, especially on design and depth.
6. Each app should be local-first, architected so cloud support can be added later without rewriting.

### Round 1


**Codex — Round 1 (skipped: CLI unavailable)**

**Claude (Design Lead · User Advocate) — Round 1**

I want to start from the screen list because I think there's a real trap waiting in this phase: everyone upstream has said "one home screen, not a dashboard," and it would be very easy for a well-meaning design pass to quietly reintroduce a dashboard by giving that one screen too much to look at. So let me lay out what I think the actual screen inventory is, and where I'd fight to keep things minimal.

Cold launch for a brand-new install goes straight into onboarding, no splash screen worth designing beyond the system launch image — this user doesn't need a logo animation, they need to get to an answer fast. Onboarding is a short sequence, not a form: a single welcome screen stating the promise in one sentence ("Cistern tells you, at a glance, whether you're okay until payday"), then an add-a-paycheck step that's really just the paycheck quick-add sheet presented inline with sensible cadence defaults already selected, then an add-a-bill step that lets you add one or several before continuing, with a visible "skip for now" that's exactly as visually weighted as "continue" — I feel strongly that skip can't look like the lesser option or we've just built a soft version of the confrontation we said we wouldn't build. Then, only after income and bills exist, an optional "want a more accurate number?" balance step with its own equally-weighted skip. Finishing onboarding — whether by completing or skipping the balance step — drops you directly onto the home screen with a real, computed runway already visible. No "you're all set!" congratulations screen in between; the answer itself is the payoff, we don't need a separate celebration screen for it.

The home screen is the one screen this app actually is. At the top, the hero: the plain-language runway sentence, the freshness framing built into that same sentence rather than a separate label, and the balance anchor as a small persistent pill that's tappable from anywhere on this screen — always visible, never scrolled out of view, because we already decided it's the number that decays fastest and burying it behind a menu would contradict that. Below the hero, I want the ambient fill/reservoir visual living as a background or supporting graphic behind or around the hero text, not a separate module competing for attention — it should read as atmosphere, like the color of the sky, not as a second data visualization the user has to interpret. Below that, and this is the part I'd watch most carefully, a compact summary — not a scrolling ledger — of what's coming next: something like "2 bills before your next paycheck" as a single tappable row that expands into the secondary bills-and-paychecks list screen, rather than the home screen itself growing a list. The moment that list starts rendering five or six rows inline on the home screen, we've rebuilt the dashboard everyone agreed to avoid. Two floating or docked quick-add entry points — add bill, add paycheck — live at the bottom of this screen, reachable without scrolling.

Below home, secondary and modal: the bills-and-paychecks management screen (a simple grouped list, tap to edit, swipe or button to soft-delete with the undo toast), the three quick-add sheets (paycheck, bill, balance-update) which double as edit sheets when opened on an existing item, a settings screen that I'd genuinely keep to almost nothing — appearance override if we don't just trust system dark mode, the optional app-lock toggle if that should-have gets built, an about/version row, and an entry point to the Premium stub screen, which is its own simple static screen listing the three paid capabilities with no purchase button that does anything. And one non-screen but essential piece of the inventory: the privacy cover view that swaps in over the home screen's real content the instant the scene goes inactive or background, so the app-switcher snapshot never shows the actual sentence — I'd design this as a branded, calm placeholder (the fill motif alone, no text) rather than a blank white screen, so it still feels intentional rather than like a glitch.

On states, per screen: home has to render five distinct conditions and they need to look and read differently, not just swap a number — zero-data (no bills, no paychecks: an inviting empty state, "Add your first paycheck to get a real answer," with the quick-add button for paycheck emphasized over bill since income is the more foundational entry); bills-only (bills exist, no paycheck scheduled: the sentence explicitly says no income is assumed rather than silently computing against a phantom payday); fresh-and-computed (the normal case); stale (crossing whatever threshold we set, the sentence's own phrasing shifts — "as of what you told me 9 days ago" — plus the ambient fill visual desaturates or dims slightly, so staleness is felt in the atmosphere of the screen, not just read in a sentence someone might skim past); and a brief recompute transition state, which should never be a spinner — it should be an immediate, springy re-layout of the existing sentence and fill level, because a spinner on a local, instant operation would itself read as a small lie about how this app works. Quick-add sheets need their own states: empty/default (cadence pre-filled with a sane guess), a validation-error state for zero/negative entries that's inline and specific rather than a system alert, and a soft-confirmation state for implausibly large amounts that reads as a friendly double-check, not a warning. The bills-and-paychecks list has its own empty state distinct from home's (e.g., "no bills yet" if someone deletes everything after having some), a populated state, and the soft-delete-with-undo-toast state layered on top of it. I'd also flag one state nobody's designed yet: what home looks like if the SwiftData store itself fails to load on launch — rare, but it needs a real, calm error screen ("something went wrong opening your data" with a retry) rather than a crash or a silently empty home screen that a scared user might mistake for a real zero-balance answer.

Visual direction: I want to actively steer away from every finance-app instinct toward red/green traffic-light color coding, because that's exactly the shame-gauge this whole product exists to not be. The fill/reservoir motif should use a warm, low-contrast gradient — something closer to dusk light on water than a status indicator — where the "okay" state is simply fuller and warmer and the "tight" state is lower and cooler, a continuous feeling rather than a binary pass/fail color swap. Typography needs a large, humane, highly legible face for the hero sentence — this is the one place in the whole app where type size and weight are doing the emotional work, so it needs to survive every Dynamic Type size without truncating or breaking into an awkward wrap; I'd design the sentence to gracefully reflow to two or three short lines at the largest accessibility sizes rather than shrinking or clipping. Supporting numerals (the balance pill, the bill amounts) can sit in a calmer, smaller, more neutral weight so they don't compete with the sentence for the eye.

Accessibility notes I'd insist survive into the final spec: the hero sentence must be exposed to VoiceOver as one coherent accessibility element with one label, not fragmented into separately-read text runs by styling views, and when a recompute happens after a quick-add, that change should post an accessibility announcement, not rely on a sighted user noticing the springy re-layout — otherwise a VoiceOver user gets no confirmation their edit actually did anything. Every tappable target — the balance pill, the two quick-add buttons, the bill/paycheck rows, the undo-toast action — needs a real 44pt hit area even where the visual glyph is smaller. Contrast on the ambient fill gradient has to be checked as an actual overlay condition, not just checked as a static swatch, because text sitting on top of a gradient that shifts by state is exactly the kind of thing that quietly fails WCAG AA in the "stale" or "tight" variant even if the "fresh" variant passes.

The biggest risk to my own proposal is scope creep in the wrong direction — that "compact summary row" on home is a real design tightrope, and if whoever builds this defaults to a scrollable list because it's easier to implement than a true single-line summary, we've quietly rebuilt the dashboard. I'd rather this phase commit explicitly to home rendering at most one hero, one fill visual, one balance pill, and one single-line "what's coming" summary row — nothing else — and push every additional piece of information behind that one tap into the secondary list screen, even if it's tempting to surface "just one more thing" on the primary screen later.

**Coordinator (Claude) — decision after round 1**

CONSENSUS: YES

Same pattern as every phase in this session so far — Codex never showed up, so it's one detailed pass from Claude, but it's a complete one that hits all five required outputs (screen inventory, primary flow, state model, visual direction, accessibility notes) with actual buildable specificity rather than vague UX language, and it doesn't contradict anything locked upstream.

The core insight driving this round: the biggest threat to "one home screen, not a dashboard" isn't a rogue new feature, it's the home screen itself quietly growing a second data visualization or a scrollable list because that's easier to build than a true single-line summary. So the design commits to a hard rule — home renders exactly one hero sentence, one ambient fill visual, one balance pill, one single-line "what's coming next" summary row, and nothing else — with everything else pushed one tap away into a secondary list screen.

The screen inventory is concrete: onboarding as a short non-form sequence (welcome → paycheck quick-add → bill quick-add, both skippable with equally-weighted skip/continue → optional balance step, also skippable) landing straight on a computed home screen with no congratulations interstitial; home as the one primary screen; a secondary bills/paychecks list screen; three quick-add sheets that double as edit sheets; a near-empty settings screen; a static Premium stub; and a privacy cover view (a calm, branded placeholder, not a blank screen) that swaps in on backgrounding so the app-switcher never leaks the runway sentence.

State modeling is genuinely thorough rather than a checkbox: home gets five distinct, differently-rendered states (zero-data, bills-only, fresh, stale, and a spinner-free springy recompute transition), quick-add sheets get default/validation-error/soft-confirmation states, the list screen gets its own empty/populated/soft-delete-with-undo states, and — new this round — a previously undesigned case: what home shows if the SwiftData store itself fails to load, resolved as a calm retry screen rather than a crash or a misleadingly empty home.

Visual direction explicitly rejects red/green traffic-light coding in favor of a warm, continuous dusk-on-water gradient tied to fullness/warmth rather than a binary pass/fail swap, with a large, humane hero typeface designed to reflow (never truncate) at max Dynamic Type sizes.

Accessibility notes go beyond the standard checklist: the hero sentence must be one coherent VoiceOver element (not fragmented by styling), recomputes need an explicit accessibility announcement so non-sighted users get confirmation their edit landed, and contrast must be checked against the gradient as an actual per-state overlay condition, not a single static swatch.

Nothing here reopens or conflicts with any locked decision (must-have list, cash-flow correctness priorities, monetization split, privacy/App Group decisions) — it's the visual/IA layer built on top of them. Both acceptance checks pass: a SwiftUI developer has enough here to build the view hierarchy, and every required workflow (onboarding, core loop, edit/delete, staleness, empty/error states) has an explicit screen/state path.

## Final Output

**Screen inventory:**
- **Onboarding** (sequence, not a form): welcome screen (one-sentence promise) → paycheck quick-add (cadence pre-filled) → bill quick-add (add one or several) → optional balance-anchor step ("want a more accurate number?"). "Skip" and "Continue" are equally visually weighted at every optional step. No completion/congratulations screen — finishing (or skipping) drops straight onto a fully computed home screen.
- **Home** (the one primary screen): hero runway sentence, ambient fill/reservoir visual as background atmosphere, a persistent tappable balance-anchor pill, a single-line "what's coming next" summary row (e.g. "2 bills before your next paycheck") that expands to the list screen, and two docked quick-add entry points (bill, paycheck) — nothing else renders on this screen.
- **Bills & paychecks list** (secondary): grouped list, tap to edit, swipe/button to soft-delete with undo toast.
- **Quick-add sheets** (paycheck, bill, balance-update): also serve as edit sheets when opened on an existing item.
- **Settings** (near-empty): appearance override (if not just trusting system dark mode), optional app-lock toggle (should-have), about/version, entry point to Premium stub.
- **Premium stub**: static screen listing the three paid capabilities (saved what-if scenarios, variable-income averaging, widget), no functional purchase button.
- **Privacy cover view** (non-navigable, system-triggered): swaps over home's real content on `scenePhase` backgrounding — a calm, branded placeholder (fill motif only, no text), not a blank screen.

**Primary user flow:** Cold launch → onboarding (income → bills → optional balance, each skippable, non-blocking) → home screen with a real computed runway visible immediately → quick-add or edit a bill/paycheck/balance from home (sheet → instant recompute → dismiss back to home) → optionally drill into the list screen for management → app backgrounds → privacy cover engages → relaunch restores exact state.

**State model per screen:**
- *Home*: zero-data (inviting empty state, paycheck CTA emphasized over bill) · bills-only (explicit "no income assumed" framing) · fresh/computed (normal case) · stale (sentence phrasing shifts + fill visual desaturates/dims past a threshold) · recompute transition (springy re-layout, never a spinner) · data-load-failure (calm "something went wrong opening your data" + retry, never a silent/empty home that could be mistaken for a real answer).
- *Quick-add sheets*: default/pre-filled · inline validation-error (zero/negative rejected) · soft-confirmation (implausibly large amount, dismissible, non-alarming).
- *List screen*: empty ("no bills yet") · populated · soft-delete-with-undo-toast overlay.
- *Privacy cover*: engages on background/inactive, no separate designed states beyond calm-placeholder.

**Visual direction:** No red/green shame-gauge anywhere. The fill/reservoir motif uses a warm, low-contrast, continuous gradient (dusk-on-water, not a status light) — fuller/warmer for "okay," lower/cooler for "tight," always a gradient, never a hard color swap. Large, humane hero typography carries the emotional weight of the sentence and must reflow gracefully (not truncate or shrink) at maximum Dynamic Type sizes. Supporting numerals (balance pill, bill amounts) use a calmer, smaller, neutral weight so they never compete with the hero sentence.

**Accessibility notes:** Hero sentence exposed to VoiceOver as one single coherent element, not fragmented by styling; every recompute posts an explicit accessibility announcement (not just a visual re-layout) so non-sighted users get confirmation an edit took effect; all tap targets (balance pill, quick-add buttons, list rows, undo action) meet 44pt regardless of visual glyph size; contrast on the ambient gradient is verified per-state as an actual overlay condition (fresh/stale/tight variants each checked), not just on a static swatch.

**Quality Gate (Claude) — after round 1**

The core insight driving this round: the biggest threat to "one home screen, not a dashboard" isn't a rogue new feature, it's the home screen itself quietly growing a second data visualization or a scrollable list because that's easier to build than a true single-line summary. So the design commits to a hard rule — home renders exactly one hero sentence, one ambient fill visual, one balance pill, one single-line "what's coming next" summary row, and nothing else — with everything else pushed one tap away into a secondary list screen.

Visual direction explicitly rejects red/green traffic-light coding in favor of a warm, continuous dusk-on-water gradient tied to fullness/warmth rather than a binary pass/fail swap, with a large, humane hero typeface designed to reflow (never truncate) at max Dynamic Type sizes.

Both acceptance checks pass: a SwiftUI developer has enough here to build the view hierarchy, and every required workflow (onboarding, core loop, edit/delete, staleness, empty/error states) has an explicit screen/state path.
