### Round 1


**Codex — Round 1 (skipped: CLI unavailable)**

_Codex skipped — in cooldown (rate_limit)._

**Claude (Frontend Engineer · Systems Thinker) — Round 1**

Let me come at this from the implementation side, because I think there are real engineering consequences hiding inside decisions that read as purely visual in the last phase, and if this handoff doesn't pin them down now, a builder will make different guesses than a designer would.

Start with navigation, because "one home screen, not a dashboard" is actually a navigation-architecture claim, not just a layout claim. I'd structure this as a single NavigationStack rooted at Home, with everything else layered on top of it rather than pushed alongside it: onboarding presents as a full-screen cover at first launch (not a pushed stack, so there's no back-swipe that leaves the user in a half-onboarded limbo state), the bills-and-paychecks list is a genuine NavigationLink push from the "what's coming next" row so the system back gesture works and VoiceOver's implicit "back" affordance is free, and all three quick-add/edit flows are `.sheet` presentations with a medium detent, never full-screen — full-screen would visually promote them to the same weight as Home, which contradicts the whole "home is the app" premise. Settings and the Premium stub live behind a small, deliberately unemphasized icon in the top corner of Home — not a tab bar, not a hamburger menu, just a single glyph, because the moment Home grows chrome that looks navigable, it starts reading as a dashboard again even if the content underneath obeys the one-hero rule.

On state management: I want a single `@Observable` `RunwayEngine` that owns a pure function — `func project(bills: [Bill], paychecks: [Paycheck], anchor: BalanceAnchor?, now: Date) -> RunwayProjection` — completely decoupled from SwiftData and completely decoupled from SwiftUI. That purity is what makes the mandated unit-test coverage for month-end clamping, same-day bias, and the generation-horizon cap actually testable without spinning up a persistence stack. The view layer wraps this with `@Query` on Bill/Paycheck/BalanceAnchor so recompute happens for free whenever SwiftData's context changes — no manual "recompute now" call, no spinner state to accidentally build. But I'd flag a real risk here: `@Query` observing the full Bill/Paycheck collections means any edit anywhere invalidates Home's view identity, which is fine for correctness but means the springy recompute transition needs to be driven by `.animation(_:value: projection)` keyed on the derived projection value, not on the raw query results — otherwise unrelated field touches (like a soft-delete's `deletedAt` timestamp ticking) will retrigger the whole hero-text animation even when the runway sentence itself didn't change wording.

Pay-period projection needs a memoization boundary of its own. Generating occurrences out to whatever horizon cap gets picked (I'd say 2 years, comfortably past any "few months" reasonable planning window, hard-stop regardless of cadence) is cheap once, but if it's recomputed on every SwiftUI body evaluation — which can happen many times a second — that's real, avoidable cost for no reason. I'd cache generated occurrences per entity keyed on a hash of (start date, cadence, horizon) and only regenerate when that tuple changes, not on every render pass.

The privacy cover view needs to sit one level higher than the design discussion placed it. It was scoped as swapping over "home's real content" on backgrounding, but that's not sufficient — if the user backgrounds mid-edit inside a quick-add sheet, that sheet is showing a live dollar amount too, and it's still on screen underneath the cover if the cover only targets Home. The cover has to be applied at the window/root level, above the entire presentation stack including whatever sheet or the list screen is active, triggered off `scenePhase` at the App or root-Scene level rather than inside HomeView. This is the kind of thing that's invisible in a static mock and only shows up once you think through what's actually on screen when someone backgrounds the app mid-flow.

Layout for Home under Dynamic Type: "the sentence must reflow, never truncate or clip" is a real constraint that pushes a real decision — Home can't be a fixed-height VStack pinned between safe areas, because at accessibility text sizes the hero sentence plus the balance pill plus the summary row plus two quick-add buttons will not fit in one un-scrolled screen on smaller devices. So Home is a ScrollView with the fill gradient as a full-bleed background behind the scroll content, and the content itself — hero, pill, summary row, quick-add buttons — stacked in a VStack inside it. At default text sizes it'll look and feel like a fixed hero screen because everything fits without scrolling; at max accessibility sizes it becomes genuinely scrollable rather than clipped, which is the only way to honor both "one hero screen" and "never truncate" at the same time.

Soft-delete needs a concrete mechanism, not just a UX pattern: Bill and Paycheck get a `deletedAt: Date?` field, the projection engine filters it out, the undo toast simply nils it back out within its visible window, and only after the toast expires does an actual SwiftData delete happen (or it just stays soft-deleted forever, which is honestly simpler and cheaper than a cleanup pass for an MVP this size — I'd default to that unless storage becomes a real concern, which it won't at this data scale).

Component inventory, concretely: `HeroRunwayText` (one AttributedString-backed view, single accessibility element, never composed of multiple Text views so VoiceOver doesn't fragment it), `CisternFillView` (the ambient gradient, driven by a 0–1 "fullness" value plus a "temperature" value derived from runway state, animated via `.animation(.easeInOut, value:)`, and must respect `accessibilityReduceMotion` by disabling the transition animation and just cross-fading), `BalancePill` (capsule, tappable, opens the balance quick-add sheet), `ComingNextRow` (single line, tap target the full row height even though the text is short, pushes to the list screen scrolled/highlighted to the relevant items), `QuickAddButton` ×2, a single parameterized `QuickAddSheet` that takes an `EntryKind` (bill/paycheck/balance) and doubles as the edit sheet when initialized with an existing entity, `UndoToast`, `EmptyStateView`, `BillsOnlyBanner` (or inline sentence variant), `DataLoadErrorView`, `PrivacyCoverView`, and `PremiumFeatureRow` for the stub list.

Tokens, so nobody has to guess a hex value mid-build: background base in dark mode around a deep ink (#12161C), light mode around a soft warm-white (#F7F3EC) rather than pure white, since pure white is exactly the sterile-spreadsheet feeling this app is trying to avoid. The fill gradient runs between two token pairs — a warm end (amber-to-coral, roughly #E8A65C to #E0785E) for "okay/full" and a cool end (slate-to-teal, roughly #5C7A8A to #3F5C68) for "tight/low" — always interpolated continuously by the fullness value, never swapped as discrete states. Hero typography: SF Pro Rounded (or SF Pro if Rounded reads too playful for a finance app — I'd actually lean plain SF Pro for credibility, Rounded for the fill-motif accents only), sized around 34pt at default Dynamic Type scaling normally up through accessibility sizes, with a max-width-driven line-wrap rather than a font-size ceiling so it keeps growing per Dynamic Type but wraps instead of overflowing. Body/supporting numerals sit at 17pt regular weight, captions at 13pt. Spacing on a 4pt base grid: 4/8/12/16/24/32/48. Corner radii: capsule (pill, buttons), 20pt for the toast and cards, system default for sheets. Motion: recompute transition is a spring at response ~0.45, damping ~0.8, applied to the hero text and fill value together but not to the whole screen's layout (so it feels like the number breathing, not the screen jumping); the undo toast slides up from the bottom with a fade, standard duration; sheet presentation/dismissal use the system default — no custom sheet transition, that's not where the design budget should go.

Accessibility specifics I'd add on top of what design discussion already named: after a quick-add sheet dismisses, VoiceOver focus should return to the control that opened it (the quick-add button or the list row that was being edited), and the recompute accessibility announcement should be posted after that focus change settles, not simultaneously, or the two will race and VoiceOver users will miss one of them. The skip/continue button pair in onboarding needs a consistent reading order across every step (I'd put continue first in VoiceOver order even though skip may render first or leftmost visually, since defaulting to the primary action in swipe order is what most users expect, and consistency step-to-step matters more than which specific order is chosen).

On the external-design question: I don't think this app should go through a Claude Design/Figma handoff step at all. The entire visual identity here is state-driven — the gradient is a function of live projection data, the hero text has to reflow correctly at every Dynamic Type size, the recompute motion is tied to a spring on a derived value — none of that survives translation through a static comp without the implementing engineer just reverse-engineering it back into SwiftUI anyway, which is wasted round-tripping for an app this size. I'd make the explicit call: no external design tool, build directly in SwiftUI from the token system and component inventory specified here. If someone wants App Store marketing screenshots later, that's a separate, much smaller ask and not part of this handoff.

The thing I'd most want preserved if this gets compressed later: the privacy cover has to be root-level, not Home-level, and the projection engine has to be a pure, SwiftData-free function — those are the two places where "obviously fine" instincts produce a real leak or a real untestable mess if a builder just follows the screen-by-screen description literally without the architectural note attached.

**Coordinator (Claude) — decision after round 1**

CONSENSUS: YES

## Final Output

Same pattern as every phase in this session — Codex never showed up, so it's one detailed pass from Claude, but this one closes out the design_handoff phase properly: it takes the visual/IA spec from `design_discussion` and adds the engineering layer a builder actually needs, without contradicting or re-litigating anything decided upstream. All six required outputs (screen specs, tokens, component inventory, state/interaction notes, accessibility, and the external-design decision) are present with buildable specificity.

**Navigation architecture:** Single `NavigationStack` rooted at Home. Onboarding is a full-screen cover at first launch (not pushed, so there's no back-swipe leaving a half-onboarded state). The bills/paychecks list is a real `NavigationLink` push. All three quick-add/edit flows are `.sheet` presentations at medium detent — never full-screen, since full-screen would visually promote them to Home's weight. Settings/Premium live behind one small, unemphasized icon in Home's corner — no tab bar, no hamburger menu, so Home never grows chrome that reads as a dashboard.

**State management:** A pure, SwiftData-free `RunwayEngine.project(bills:paychecks:anchor:now:) -> RunwayProjection` function is the one piece everything hinges on — it's what makes the mandated unit tests for month-end clamping, same-day bias, and the generation-horizon cap actually testable in isolation. Views wrap it with `@Query`, so recompute is automatic; the springy transition is keyed on the derived `projection` value (not raw query results) so unrelated field touches like a soft-delete timestamp don't retrigger the hero animation. Pay-period generation (2-year hard horizon) is memoized per entity, keyed on `(start date, cadence, horizon)`, not regenerated every body evaluation.

**Two corrections to the prior phase that matter architecturally:**
1. The privacy cover view has to sit at window/root level (driven by `scenePhase` at the App/root-Scene level), not inside `HomeView` — otherwise a live dollar amount in an open quick-add sheet stays visible under the cover if the user backgrounds mid-edit.
2. Home can't be a fixed, non-scrolling VStack — at max Dynamic Type sizes, the hero sentence plus pill plus summary row plus buttons won't fit on smaller devices. It's a `ScrollView` with the fill gradient full-bleed behind the content, so it looks and behaves like a fixed hero screen at normal sizes and becomes genuinely scrollable (never clipped) at accessibility sizes — the only way to honor both "one hero screen" and "never truncate."

**Soft-delete mechanism:** `deletedAt: Date?` on Bill/Paycheck, filtered out by the projection engine, nil'd back by undo; no cleanup pass needed at this data scale.

**Component inventory:** `HeroRunwayText` (single accessibility element, never multiple `Text` views), `CisternFillView` (gradient driven by fullness + temperature, respects `accessibilityReduceMotion` by cross-fading instead of animating), `BalancePill`, `ComingNextRow`, `QuickAddButton` ×2, one parameterized `QuickAddSheet` (bill/paycheck/balance, doubles as edit), `UndoToast`, `EmptyStateView`, `BillsOnlyBanner`, `DataLoadErrorView`, `PrivacyCoverView`, `PremiumFeatureRow`.

**Design tokens:** Dark base `#12161C`, light base `#F7F3EC` (not pure white — avoids the sterile-spreadsheet feel). Fill gradient interpolates continuously between warm/full (`#E8A65C`→`#E0785E`) and cool/tight (`#5C7A8A`→`#3F5C68`) — never a discrete state swap. Hero type: plain SF Pro (Rounded reserved for fill-motif accents only) at ~34pt, wrapping by max-width rather than font-size ceiling. Body/numerals 17pt regular, captions 13pt. Spacing on a 4pt grid (4/8/12/16/24/32/48). Radii: capsule for pills/buttons, 20pt for toast/cards, system default for sheets. Motion: recompute is a spring (response ~0.45, damping ~0.8) applied to hero text and fill value only, not the whole layout; undo toast slides up with a fade; sheets use system-default transitions.

**Accessibility additions on top of `design_discussion`'s notes:** VoiceOver focus returns to the triggering control after a sheet dismisses, and the recompute announcement posts after that focus settles (not simultaneously, to avoid a race); skip/continue in onboarding keep a consistent VoiceOver reading order (continue first) across every step regardless of visual placement.

**External design decision (explicit, as the phase requires):** No Claude Design/Figma handoff. The entire visual identity is state-driven (gradient as a function of live projection data, reflow-at-every-Dynamic-Type-size hero text, spring motion tied to a derived value) — none of that survives a static comp without being reverse-engineered back into SwiftUI anyway. Build directly from this token system and component inventory. Marketing screenshots, if wanted later, are a separate, smaller task outside this handoff.

Both acceptance checks pass: a SwiftUI builder has enough here (navigation structure, state-ownership boundaries, memoization strategy, concrete tokens, component list, accessibility specifics) to implement without guessing, and nothing here depends on a hidden decision — the two gaps that would have bitten a literal-minded builder (privacy cover scope, Dynamic Type layout) are explicitly closed. Nothing contradicts any locked decision from prior phases; this is purely the implementation layer on top of the approved IA/visual direction. Design handoff is complete.

## Final Output

Same pattern as every phase in this session — Codex never showed up, so it's one detailed pass from Claude, but this one closes out the design_handoff phase properly: it takes the visual/IA spec from `design_discussion` and adds the engineering layer a builder actually needs, without contradicting or re-litigating anything decided upstream. All six required outputs (screen specs, tokens, component inventory, state/interaction notes, accessibility, and the external-design decision) are present with buildable specificity.

**Navigation architecture:** Single `NavigationStack` rooted at Home. Onboarding is a full-screen cover at first launch (not pushed, so there's no back-swipe leaving a half-onboarded state). The bills/paychecks list is a real `NavigationLink` push. All three quick-add/edit flows are `.sheet` presentations at medium detent — never full-screen, since full-screen would visually promote them to Home's weight. Settings/Premium live behind one small, unemphasized icon in Home's corner — no tab bar, no hamburger menu, so Home never grows chrome that reads as a dashboard.

**State management:** A pure, SwiftData-free `RunwayEngine.project(bills:paychecks:anchor:now:) -> RunwayProjection` function is the one piece everything hinges on — it's what makes the mandated unit tests for month-end clamping, same-day bias, and the generation-horizon cap actually testable in isolation. Views wrap it with `@Query`, so recompute is automatic; the springy transition is keyed on the derived `projection` value (not raw query results) so unrelated field touches like a soft-delete timestamp don't retrigger the hero animation. Pay-period generation (2-year hard horizon) is memoized per entity, keyed on `(start date, cadence, horizon)`, not regenerated every body evaluation.

**Two corrections to the prior phase that matter architecturally:**
1. The privacy cover view has to sit at window/root level (driven by `scenePhase` at the App/root-Scene level), not inside `HomeView` — otherwise a live dollar amount in an open quick-add sheet stays visible under the cover if the user backgrounds mid-edit.
2. Home can't be a fixed, non-scrolling VStack — at max Dynamic Type sizes, the hero sentence plus pill plus summary row plus buttons won't fit on smaller devices. It's a `ScrollView` with the fill gradient full-bleed behind the content, so it looks and behaves like a fixed hero screen at normal sizes and becomes genuinely scrollable (never clipped) at accessibility sizes — the only way to honor both "one hero screen" and "never truncate."

**Soft-delete mechanism:** `deletedAt: Date?` on Bill/Paycheck, filtered out by the projection engine, nil'd back by undo; no cleanup pass needed at this data scale.

**Component inventory:** `HeroRunwayText` (single accessibility element, never multiple `Text` views), `CisternFillView` (gradient driven by fullness + temperature, respects `accessibilityReduceMotion` by cross-fading instead of animating), `BalancePill`, `ComingNextRow`, `QuickAddButton` ×2, one parameterized `QuickAddSheet` (bill/paycheck/balance, doubles as edit), `UndoToast`, `EmptyStateView`, `BillsOnlyBanner`, `DataLoadErrorView`, `PrivacyCoverView`, `PremiumFeatureRow`.

**Design tokens:** Dark base `#12161C`, light base `#F7F3EC` (not pure white — avoids the sterile-spreadsheet feel). Fill gradient interpolates continuously between warm/full (`#E8A65C`→`#E0785E`) and cool/tight (`#5C7A8A`→`#3F5C68`) — never a discrete state swap. Hero type: plain SF Pro (Rounded reserved for fill-motif accents only) at ~34pt, wrapping by max-width rather than font-size ceiling. Body/numerals 17pt regular, captions 13pt. Spacing on a 4pt grid (4/8/12/16/24/32/48). Radii: capsule for pills/buttons, 20pt for toast/cards, system default for sheets. Motion: recompute is a spring (response ~0.45, damping ~0.8) applied to hero text and fill value only, not the whole layout; undo toast slides up with a fade; sheets use system-default transitions.

**Accessibility additions on top of `design_discussion`'s notes:** VoiceOver focus returns to the triggering control after a sheet dismisses, and the recompute announcement posts after that focus settles (not simultaneously, to avoid a race); skip/continue in onboarding keep a consistent VoiceOver reading order (continue first) across every step regardless of visual placement.

**External design decision (explicit, as the phase requires):** No Claude Design/Figma handoff. The entire visual identity is state-driven (gradient as a function of live projection data, reflow-at-every-Dynamic-Type-size hero text, spring motion tied to a derived value) — none of that survives a static comp without being reverse-engineered back into SwiftUI anyway. ## Parent Portfolio Prompt

Build 7 completely separate, production-ready iOS apps at the same time.

This is a multi-app PORTFOLIO request: multiple apps in one program. Each selected app becomes its own separate project — one folder per app, never bundled together.

Pick any 7 categories/concepts you like — there is no fixed category assignment. Choose the 7 strongest ideas.

TOP PRIORITY — DESIGN & UI/UX (this outweighs every other consideration):
- Design and UI/UX quality is the single most important success criterion. An app that works but looks generic is a FAILURE here.
- Each app must have a world-class, distinctive visual identity: a deliberate color system, type scale, spacing system, iconography, and motion language — not stock SwiftUI defaults.
- Premium, polished, "Apple Design Award" caliber. Every screen considered: empty states, loading states, error states, transitions.

- Strong visual hierarchy, generous whitespace, real content density decisions, dark mode, Dynamic Type, and full accessibility (VoiceOver labels, 44pt targets, WCAG AA contrast).
- Each of the 7 apps must look and feel clearly DIFFERENT from the other 6 — seven distinct design directions, not a template recolored.
- Include a documented design system per app (tokens, components, states) and make the built UI actually match it.

SECOND PRIORITY — COMPLEXITY & DEPTH (required):
- Each app must be genuinely COMPLEX and substantial — NOT a simple single-purpose utility or a weekend toy.
- Expect many interconnected features and screens, a real domain data model with relationships, non-trivial business logic and algorithms, meaningful state management, background work, and offline persistence with a clear sync-later architecture.
- Depth that rewards long-term daily use: robust settings, edge-case handling, empty/error/loading states everywhere, data import/export, and at least one genuinely hard technical capability done well.
- Aim for the scope a small team would build over months, not something trivial. Complexity must serve real user value — never complexity for its own sake.

THIRD PRIORITY — MONETIZATION (required):
- Each app must have a clear, sustainable monetization strategy integrated into the product.
- Each app should have a realistic path to revenue: subscription tiers with genuine value-add features, IAP (in-app purchases) for premium features or content, premium versions vs. lite, ad networks (only if user experience respects the user), or a combination.
- Monetization must NOT feel tacked-on or punitive. It must align with the product's core value proposition and user expectations.
- Design the free tier to hook users and deliver real value (so they want to upgrade); design paid tiers to be compelling, not required for basic functionality.
- Estimate realistic pricing (what would users actually pay, what competitors charge, what the market supports).

1. EXACTLY ONE app must be an IDLE GAME (incremental/clicker style). Make it premium-designed and deeply monetized (subscription pass, cosmetics IAP, battle pass, seasonal content).

3. The other 5 apps can be ANY category (productivity, utility, social, wellness, creative, finance, education, etc.) — just ensure diversity, complexity, design excellence, and clear monetization.

General Requirements:
1. Each app must go from 0 to production-ready, not just a few features.
2. Each app must be unique, useful, and commercially viable.
3. Each app must provide real end-user value.
4. Each app should have viral potential (broad or niche) without sacrificing usefulness.
5. Each app should be better than its competitors in a meaningful way, especially on design and depth.
6. Each app should be local-first, architected so cloud support can be added later without rewriting.

UNIQUENESS (hard rule, non-negotiable):
- No app in this batch may be similar to another app in this batch.
- No app may be similar to anything already built in this workspace or before (Waylay, TrueScale, Provenance, Scope-Guard, Practice-Loop, Proof, ReturnWise, VerveCoach, CueKeeper, brinekeeper, chalkline, quietpilot, and the ~50+ apps of prior batches).

## Transcript


### Round 1


**Codex — Round 1 (skipped: CLI unavailable)**

_Codex skipped — in cooldown (rate_limit)._

**Claude (QA & Risk · Closer) — Round 1**

I want to close out the architecture-level gaps before this goes to tech_specs, because a few things that read as "obviously fine" in the design_handoff phase are actually concrete iOS platform decisions nobody has pinned down yet, and I'd rather lock them now than have tech_specs discover them mid-implementation.

Start with the SwiftUI architecture recommendation, which I think is basically already right and I want to ratify rather than reopen: MV, not MVVM — `@Query` directly in views backed by the pure `RunwayEngine.project(...)` function already locked in design_handoff, no view-model layer in between. That's the correct call for an app this size, and adding an MVVM layer here would be complexity for its own sake. One concrete addition though: the `@Query` filters for Bill and Paycheck need to use the `#Predicate` macro to exclude soft-deleted rows at the fetch layer — `@Query(filter: #Predicate<Bill> { $0.deletedAt == nil })` — not fetched-then-filtered-in-Swift, because that's both simpler and means a stale soft-deleted row can never leak into the projection engine by accident.

The single most dangerous unstated engineering decision in this whole app is date arithmetic, and I want to make it explicit and non-negotiable: all cadence math — biweekly, semimonthly, monthly generation, "days until" calculations — has to go through `Calendar.date(byAdding:to:)` using the device's autoupdating current calendar, never raw `TimeInterval`/epoch-second arithmetic. Adding `86400 * 14` seconds to compute "two weeks from now" silently breaks the day a DST transition sits inside that window — the user gets a paycheck that's off by an hour's worth of a day, which sounds trivial until it's the difference between a bill landing before or after a payday in the projection. This has been named abstractly as a risk since the prompt contract, but nobody has said "use Calendar, not TimeInterval" as an actual engineering rule, so I'm saying it now: that's the rule, and it needs to show up as a code-review-blocking constraint in tech_specs, not just a test case.

On Apple framework choices: SwiftData for persistence, Observation for state, LocalAuthentication only if the should-have Face ID/passcode lock gets built, and that's it — no WidgetKit target, no StoreKit imports anywhere in this codebase, no CloudKit. I want to be blunt about StoreKit specifically because I think there's a real temptation trap in the Premium stub: someone building this is going to be tempted to wire up a `.storekit` configuration file and a real (if sandboxed) purchase button "since it's easy," and that is scope creep past what next_steps_small and app_features already decided. The Premium screen is static text, zero StoreKit import, full stop — if that changes, it's a new decision requiring explicit sign-off, not something that drifts in because it was convenient to add.

Persistence plan, concretely: `ModelConfiguration` targeting the App Group container (`group.<bundle-id>.shared`, decide the literal string now so it's not improvised at build time), `cloudKitDatabase: .none` since there's no sync this pass. The one thing I want to add on top of what's already locked: define a `VersionedSchema` and an (even if trivial, single-version) `SchemaMigrationPlan` from day one, not just a bare `@Model` schema. This is the same argument that already won the App Group debate — cheap now, and it means the first time this app's data model actually changes (adding the widget, adding what-if scenarios for Premium, adding CloudKit), there's already a migration plan shape to extend instead of a bare schema that's never been versioned and needs to be retrofitted under pressure with real user data on the line. I'd also want the `ModelContainer` construction to live in one shared, static factory function (not inlined in the App struct) specifically so a future widget extension target can call the identical factory and guarantee it's pointed at the same container with the same schema — that's the actual mechanism that makes "widget-ready" true rather than aspirational.

Permissions and privacy: this app requests exactly one permission, conditionally — Face ID, only if the should-have lock ships, with an `NSFaceIDUsageDescription` string that's honest and calm ("Used to keep your financial information private"), and a graceful path when biometrics aren't enrolled on the device — the toggle should just not be offered rather than erroring, and Settings shouldn't dead-end into a broken state. No camera, no location, no contacts, no push notification permission, no network permission dialog because there's no network entitlement at all. One concrete deliverable I don't think anyone's named yet: Apple now requires a `PrivacyInfo.xcprivacy` manifest at submission time, and this app's is genuinely easy to get right — zero collected data types, and if UserDefaults is used anywhere (onboarding-complete flag, appearance override), the required-reason API declaration for UserDefaults access needs to be in that manifest or the build gets flagged in App Store Connect. I want this named now as a build-phase checklist item, not discovered at submission time.

Dependency and license decision: zero third-party dependencies, Swift Package Manager unused entirely for this pass. I'll flag the one place this could be quietly violated: someone reaching for a snapshot-testing library (like swift-snapshot-testing) to verify the five home states visually. I don't think that's needed — Xcode's built-in `XCTAttachment`-based screenshot capture inside XCUITest, plus SwiftUI Previews for manual state verification, covers this without adding a dependency, and I'd close the door on it explicitly rather than let it sneak in as "just a test-only dependency."

Testing implications, and this is where I want to be a closer rather than gesture at "unit tests required": the `RunwayEngine` test suite needs to explicitly enumerate, as separate test cases, every edge case already named in detailed_discussion — semimonthly cadence with the 15th on a weekend, semimonthly with the 1st falling on Feb 29/30/31, biweekly generation hitting the two-year horizon cap and stopping, a bill dated the 31st clamped into a 30-day month, a paycheck dated the 31st clamped the same way, the same-day-bill-already-subtracted rule, negative/zero rejection at the model layer, and the bills-only-no-income state producing the correct honest projection. If tech_specs doesn't carry this exact list forward as named test cases, it'll get built as "some tests" and that's not good enough for the one part of this app that isn't allowed to be wrong. Alongside that, SwiftData persistence itself needs an in-memory `ModelConfiguration` test harness (`isStoredInMemoryOnly: true`) so persistence and migration logic are testable without touching disk. And I want to flag a real testability gotcha for whoever builds the Face ID should-have: LocalAuthentication doesn't have enrolled biometrics on a fresh simulator by default, and testing it requires enabling Face ID/Touch ID simulation through the simulator's Features menu — that's not obvious, and a builder who doesn't know it will think the feature is broken when it's actually a simulator configuration step.

Last thing, stated as an explicit decision so the acceptance check ("any ML, LLM, or AR use is justified by user value") isn't left ambiguous: this app uses none of those, and that's correct, not a gap. Nothing about a calm, single-number cash-flow answer benefits from ML or AR, and forcing one in would violate the "complexity must serve real user value" principle from the very top of this whole project. Same for background work — no `BGTaskScheduler`, no background refresh, no push registration — there is nothing that needs to run when the app isn't open, since there are no notifications and no sync in this pass.

The one place I'd push back on my own instinct here: the versioned-schema-and-migration-plan requirement might look like premature engineering for an MVP that's explicitly supposed to be small. I don't think it is, for the same reason the App Group decision already isn't — a schema migration on a device holding someone's real financial data, done for the first time under a future feature-launch deadline, is a much worse time to learn the migration APIs than now, before any user data exists. If this phase is going to draw one hard line, that's the one I'd draw: App Group container, versioned schema, and Calendar-based (never epoch-based) date math are non-negotiable architecture requirements for tech_specs to carry forward as-is, not something to be simplified away if the build phase feels time pressure.

**Apple framework choices:** SwiftData, Observation, LocalAuthentication (only if the should-have Face ID lock ships). Explicitly no WidgetKit target, no StoreKit, no CloudKit. **Persistence plan:** `ModelConfiguration` targeting the App Group container (literal group ID to be fixed now, not improvised at build time), `cloudKitDatabase: .none`. New this round: a `VersionedSchema` + `SchemaMigrationPlan` from day one (even trivially single-version), and the `ModelContainer` built from one shared static factory function so a future widget target calls the identical factory — the concrete mechanism that actually makes "widget-ready" true rather than aspirational.

**Permissions/privacy:** exactly one conditional permission (Face ID, only if built), honest `NSFaceIDUsageDescription`, graceful no-biometrics-enrolled fallback. No camera/location/contacts/push/network permissions at all. Flags a genuinely new, concrete deliverable nobody had named yet: the `PrivacyInfo.xcprivacy` manifest required at App Store submission — zero collected data types, plus a required-reason API declaration if UserDefaults is used anywhere (onboarding flag, appearance override). This gets named as a build-phase checklist item now rather than a submission-time surprise.

**Dependency/license decision:** zero third-party dependencies, SwiftPM unused. Explicitly closes the door on reaching for a snapshot-testing library to verify the five home states — Xcode's built-in screenshot capture via XCUITest plus SwiftUI Previews covers it without adding a dependency.

**Testing implications:** the `RunwayEngine` suite must carry forward the exact edge-case list from `detailed_discussion` as named test cases (semimonthly-on-weekend, [REDACTED:high_entropy], biweekly hitting the horizon cap, bill and paycheck 31st-clamping, same-day-bias, negative/zero rejection, bills-only honesty) rather than being built as vague "some tests." SwiftData needs an in-memory `ModelConfiguration` test harness. Flags a real, easy-to-miss simulator gotcha: Face ID testing requires manually enabling biometric simulation via the simulator's Features menu, or a builder will wrongly think the feature is broken.

**ML/LLM/AR and background work:** explicitly none, and that's correct rather than a gap — nothing about a calm, single-number answer benefits from ML or AR, and there's no background work needed since there's no sync or notifications this pass.

**The one hard line drawn for tech_specs to carry forward as-is:** App Group container, versioned schema/migration plan, and Calendar-based (never epoch-based) date math are non-negotiable, not something to simplify away under time pressure — for the same reason as the App Group decision itself: a first-time schema migration or a DST bug discovered on a device holding someone's real financial data is a much worse time to learn this than now.

All six required outputs for this phase (SwiftUI architecture, framework choices, persistence plan, permissions/privacy plan, dependency/license decision, testing implications) are present with concrete, buildable specificity, and both acceptance checks pass — no vague architecture gaps, and no ML/LLM/AR is used at all (correctly, since none would serve real user value here), no dependency violates the permissive-license policy since there are zero dependencies. Nothing here reopens anything locked in design_handoff or earlier phases. Calling this converged.

### Round 1


**Codex — Round 1 (skipped: CLI unavailable)**

**Claude (Frontend Engineer · Visionary) — Round 1**

I want to push this phase past "here's the file layout" and actually name the one genuinely hard technical capability this app can do exceptionally well, because I think tech_specs is the last chance to decide that on purpose rather than let it fall out of whatever's easiest to code under time pressure. The obvious candidate is the `RunwayEngine` projection itself, and I think the ambitious move is to build it as a real event-merge algorithm rather than a naive day-by-day simulation loop. Concretely: generate two sorted streams of dated cash events — bill occurrences (negative) and paycheck occurrences (positive) — out to the two-year horizon, merge them by date with a single sweep, and produce a running balance curve as a byproduct, not an afterthought. That running curve is what lets `fullness` in `CisternFillView` be a continuous, mathematically real value instead of a hand-wavy heuristic, and it's also exactly the data structure a future "compare two what-if scenarios" premium feature would need — so building it properly now costs nothing extra and pays for itself twice. A brute-force "loop over every day from now to two years and subtract what's due" would also work and would be easier to write test assertions against superficially, but it's O(horizon-days) instead of O(occurrences), it invites off-by-one bugs at every single day boundary instead of concentrating the hard logic in one date-generation function, and it doesn't naturally produce the balance-curve data structure. I want to be honest that this is more code than the naive version and it's the one place I'd actually spend the "visionary" budget in an otherwise deliberately small MVP — but I think it's justified because it's the same correctness-critical code path that's already been flagged since the prompt contract as the place this app can't lie, so making it structurally clean is a correctness investment, not a nice-to-have.

On architecture overall, I'm not reopening anything locked in `ios_architecture_review` — MV over MVVM, `@Query` with `#Predicate` soft-delete exclusion, App Group container, versioned schema, Calendar-based date math, zero dependencies. What I want to add is the concrete shape of the module layout so parallel lanes don't collide: a `Cistern` target organized as `Models/` (the three `@Model` classes plus the `Cadence` enum), `Engine/` (the pure `RunwayEngine`, `RunwayProjection`, and the date-generation sweep, entirely free of SwiftData and SwiftUI imports so it compiles and tests standalone), `Persistence/` (the App Group constant, `VersionedSchema`, `SchemaMigrationPlan`, and the shared `ModelContainerFactory`), `Views/Home/`, `Views/Onboarding/`, `Views/List/`, `Views/QuickAdd/`, `Views/Settings/`, `Views/Shared/` (privacy cover, undo toast, empty/error states — the cross-cutting polish pieces), and `Support/` for the design tokens (colors, type, spacing) as a single source of truth so nobody hand-types a hex value in a view file. Two test targets: `CisternTests` for the engine and persistence, `CisternUITests` only if there's time for the five-home-state screenshot pass already scoped in the architecture review.

On the data model specifics that matter for cross-lane compatibility: `Bill` and `Paycheck` both carry `id: UUID`, `amount: Decimal` (never `Double` — this is money, and `Decimal` is the only type that won't quietly introduce binary-floating-point drift into a runway number that's supposed to be trustworthy), a `cadence: Cadence`, a reference date field, `createdAt`, and `deletedAt: Date?` for the soft-delete already locked. Semimonthly needs a second reference field — `secondAnchorDate: Date?` — because "1st and 15th" is fundamentally two independent monthly cadences sharing one entity, not one cadence with a single anchor; trying to derive the second date from the first algorithmically (e.g. "anchor plus 14 days") would silently misrepresent what semimonthly actually means to a bank. `BalanceAnchor` is `amount: Decimal`, `asOf: Date` (the date the user is asserting this balance was true, which is what freshness is actually measured against, not `createdAt`), and I'd append new anchors rather than mutate one row in place — always read the most recent by `asOf`, since keeping history costs nothing at this scale and means a future "what did I think my runway was two weeks ago" feature isn't blocked by having thrown the old number away.

Persistence: I'm not going to relitigate the App Group/versioned-schema/factory-function decisions, just pin the literal identifier now so it's not improvised mid-build — `group.com.cistern.shared` — and note that `RunwayEngine` itself takes plain arrays of already-fetched, already-filtered model data plus a `Calendar` and a `now: Date` as parameters, never touches `ModelContext` directly, which is what makes it something a test can call with fabricated arrays and zero SwiftData setup at all.

Testing strategy: the engine test suite needs the full named edge-case list from the architecture review as literal `XCTestCase` methods — I'd insist on one test per named scenario rather than parameterized loops that could silently skip a case if someone "simplifies" the test later. On top of those, I'd add explicit tests for the sweep-merge itself: an empty-bills stream, an empty-paychecks stream, and interleaved same-date bill-and-paycheck events (does the same-day-bill-already-subtracted rule still hold when a paycheck also lands that same day — the order of operations on a tied date is exactly the kind of thing a naive implementation gets backwards). Persistence tests run against an in-memory `ModelConfiguration` and verify the `#Predicate` soft-delete filter actually excludes deleted rows from a live fetch, not just from a manually filtered array.

The biggest risk to my own proposal: a sweep-line merge algorithm is a more sophisticated piece of code than a straightforward day-by-day loop, and "more sophisticated" is exactly the kind of thing that can go wrong in a way that's harder to spot in review than a simple loop would be. My answer to that risk is that the test list above is non-negotiable specifically because of this choice — if the build phase can't deliver both the sweep algorithm and its full test coverage in the time available, the fallback should be the boring day-by-day loop, correct and simple, rather than a half-tested clever version. I'd rather this phase say explicitly: attempt the event-merge approach, but the day-by-day fallback is pre-approved and not a failure if correctness under full test coverage is at risk.

One more thing I want preserved verbatim into the final spec: `RunwayProjection` needs a `dailyBalances` (or at least start/end of runway) output even though the current UI doesn't chart it, because the fill visual's `fullness` value and the eventual premium what-if-scenario feature both want a continuous balance-over-time signal, and computing it as a byproduct of the sweep costs nothing extra right now versus retrofitting it onto a scalar-only engine later.

CONSENSUS: YES

## Final Output

**Architecture overview:** MV pattern (no MVVM layer), `@Query` in views backed by a pure, SwiftData-free `RunwayEngine.project(...)`. The one deliberate technical investment: `RunwayEngine` is built as a sweep-merge algorithm over two sorted date streams (bill occurrences, paycheck occurrences) rather than a day-by-day simulation loop, producing a sparse `dailyBalances` curve as a byproduct — this is the substrate for `CisternFillView`'s continuous `fullness` value today and the future what-if/premium feature later, at no extra cost now. A boring day-by-day fallback is pre-approved if the sweep's full test coverage can't be delivered in the available time — correctness under full test coverage always wins over algorithmic sophistication.

**File/module layout:** `Models/` (the three `@Model` classes, `Cadence`), `Engine/` (pure `RunwayEngine`, `RunwayProjection`, date-generation sweep — zero SwiftData/SwiftUI imports, compiles and tests standalone), `Persistence/` (App Group constant, `VersionedSchema`, `SchemaMigrationPlan`, shared `ModelContainerFactory`), `Views/Home/`, `Views/Onboarding/`, `Views/List/`, `Views/QuickAdd/`, `Views/Settings/`, `Views/Shared/` (privacy cover, undo toast, empty/error/bills-only states), `Support/` (design tokens as single source of truth). Two test targets: `CisternTests` (engine + persistence), `CisternUITests` (five-home-state screenshot pass, time permitting).

**Data models:** `Bill`/`Paycheck` share shape — `id: UUID`, `amount: Decimal`, `cadence: Cadence`, `anchorDate`, `secondAnchorDate: Date?` (semimonthly's independent second anchor), `createdAt`, `deletedAt: Date?`. `BalanceAnchor` is `amount: Decimal`, `asOf: Date` (freshness and projection start point are measured from `asOf`, not `createdAt`), append-only history. `RunwayProjection` carries `status` (zeroData/billsOnly/fresh/stale), `runwayEndDate`, `daysRemaining`, `fullness`, `freshnessDays`, `narrativeSentence`, and `dailyBalances: [DailyBalancePoint]`.

**Persistence strategy:** `ModelConfiguration` targeting App Group `group.com.cistern.shared`, `cloudKitDatabase: .none`, `VersionedSchema`/`SchemaMigrationPlan` from day one (single version, empty migration stages — shape exists for future use), built via one shared static `ModelContainerFactory.makeContainer(inMemory:)` so a future widget target calls the identical factory. Soft-deletes excluded at the fetch layer via `#Predicate`, never filtered post-fetch in Swift.

**Testing strategy:** `RunwayEngine` suite implements the full named edge-case list from `detailed_discussion`/`ios_architecture_review` as literal `XCTestCase` methods (semimonthly-on-weekend, Feb 29/30/31, biweekly horizon-cap, 31st-clamping for both bills and paychecks, same-day-bias, negative/zero rejection, bills-only honesty), plus new sweep-specific cases (empty bill stream, empty paycheck stream, same-date bill-and-paycheck tie-breaking). Persistence tests use in-memory `ModelConfiguration` and verify the `#Predicate` soft-delete exclusion at the live-fetch level, not just on a manually filtered array.

**Quality Gate (Claude) — after round 1**

QUALITY: PASS

## Feedback

This tech_specs output clears the bar. All six required outputs are present with genuine buildable specificity, not gestures:

- **Architecture overview**: makes a real, defended engineering choice (sweep-merge event-stream algorithm over a naive day-by-day loop) tied directly to correctness value (produces a `dailyBalances` curve as a byproduct, feeding both the fill visual and the future premium what-if feature), with an explicit, sensible pre-approved fallback (boring day-by-day loop) if full test coverage of the sweep is at risk. This is exactly the kind of engineering judgment call the phase needs — ambition in service of the one thing that can't be wrong, not ambition for its own sake.
- **File/module layout**: gives parallel build lanes clean boundaries (`Models/`, `Engine/`, `Persistence/`, `Views/*`, `Support/`), with `Engine/` explicitly kept SwiftData/SwiftUI-free so it's independently testable — consistent with the pure-function requirement locked in `design_handoff` and `ios_architecture_review`.
- **Data models**: `Decimal` for money (correctly rejecting `Double`), `secondAnchorDate` as an explicit second field for semimonthly rather than derived arithmetic (well-reasoned — "1st and 15th" are two independent anchors), `BalanceAnchor` as append-only history keyed on `asOf` rather than `createdAt`. All consistent with prior phases' month-end clamping and freshness rules.
- **Persistence strategy**: pins the literal App Group identifier (`group.com.cistern.shared`) via a single `AppGroupIdentifier` source of truth, carries forward `VersionedSchema`/`SchemaMigrationPlan`/shared factory function from `ios_architecture_review` without re-litigating it, and correctly keeps `#Predicate`-based soft-delete exclusion at the fetch layer.
- **Testing strategy**: carries forward the full named edge-case list from `detailed_discussion`/`ios_architecture_review` as literal test methods (not parameterized loops that could silently drop a case), and adds new sweep-specific cases (empty streams, same-date tie-breaking) that hadn't been named before — this is genuinely additive, not just restating upstream decisions.
- **interfaces-json**: comprehensive and internally consistent — covers all four owning_lanes, includes cross-cutting types the initial round-1 draft missed (`BalancePill`, `ComingNextRow`, `QuickAddButton`, `SettingsView`, `PremiumStubView`, `PremiumFeatureRow`, `BillsOnlyBanner`, `AmountValidation`/`validateAmount`, `AppGroupIdentifier`), which the coordinator correctly caught and closed before finalizing rather than leaving for lanes to invent divergently.

Both acceptance checks pass: parallel build lanes have a shared, sufficiently complete type contract to work from without inventing incompatible types, and the spec names concretely how the app will be built (module boundaries, pure engine, factory pattern) and verified (named unit tests, in-memory persistence harness, screenshot pass for the five home states).

One pre-existing minor gap, not introduced here and not blocking: the exact staleness threshold (day-count where `fresh` flips to `stale`) is still not pinned to a literal number in `RunwayProjection.freshnessDays` handling — this was already flagged as a fast build-time fix in `design_handoff`'s quality gate (anchor to the "3+ weeks" QA bar from `per_app_product_brief`), and tech_specs doesn't need to re-decide it since it's a constant, not an architectural concern. Fine to leave for the build phase to hardcode.

Nothing here contradicts any locked decision from `prompt_contract` through `ios_architecture_review`. Good to close.

See the coordinator's message above.

## Final Output

Round 1 landed a genuinely strong, complete tech_specs pass — same pattern as every phase in this session (Codex still in cooldown, so it's one voice), but this one earns the "visionary" framing it claimed: it doesn't just restate the architecture already locked in `ios_architecture_review`, it makes one deliberate, well-justified engineering bet — building `RunwayEngine` as a sorted-stream sweep-merge algorithm that produces a real balance curve as a byproduct, rather than a naive day-by-day simulation loop — and it explicitly pre-approves falling back to the boring day-by-day loop if full test coverage of the sweep can't be delivered in time. That's the right way to spend ambition in an app whose one hard rule is "never be confidently wrong."

The module layout (`Models/`, `Engine/`, `Persistence/`, `Views/*`, `Support/`) gives parallel build lanes clean boundaries, and the data model choices are the right calls: `Decimal` (never `Double`) for money, `secondAnchorDate` as an explicit second field for semimonthly rather than derived arithmetic (correctly reasoned — "1st and 15th" is two independent anchors, not one anchor plus an offset), and `BalanceAnchor` as an append-only history rather than a mutated singleton, which costs nothing now and keeps a future "what did my runway look like two weeks ago" feature unblocked. Persistence pins the literal App Group identifier and keeps `RunwayEngine` fully SwiftData-free, which is exactly what `ios_architecture_review` demanded. Testing strategy carries forward the full named edge-case list as literal test methods (not parameterized loops that could silently drop a case) and adds new sweep-specific tests — empty streams, same-date bill/paycheck tie-breaking — that hadn't been named before.

The one gap I'm closing on the way out rather than kicking back for another round: the submitted interfaces-json was missing a handful of components that `design_handoff`'s component inventory already named (BalancePill, ComingNextRow, QuickAddButton, SettingsView, the Premium stub screen, BillsOnlyBanner) and had no concrete signature for where amount-validation (reject negative/zero, soft-confirm large) actually lives. Since the acceptance check here is specifically "parallel build lanes can work without inventing incompatible types," I'm adding those now rather than let two lanes invent divergent versions independently. Nothing else changes.

No open disagreement exists — there's no second voice to test this against, but nothing here contradicts the prompt contract, product brief, design handoff, or architecture review, and all five required outputs (architecture overview, file/module layout, data models, persistence strategy, testing strategy) plus the interfaces-json contract are present with real, buildable specificity.

CONSENSUS: YES

## Final Output

**Architecture overview:** MV pattern (no MVVM layer), `@Query` in views backed by a pure, SwiftData-free `RunwayEngine.project(...)`. The one deliberate technical investment: `RunwayEngine` is built as a sweep-merge algorithm over two sorted date streams (bill occurrences, paycheck occurrences) rather than a day-by-day simulation loop, producing a sparse `dailyBalances` curve as a byproduct — this is the substrate for `CisternFillView`'s continuous `fullness` value today and the future what-if/premium feature later, at no extra cost now. A boring day-by-day fallback is pre-approved if the sweep's full test coverage can't be delivered in the available time — correctness under full test coverage always wins over algorithmic sophistication.

**File/module layout:** `Models/` (the three `@Model` classes, `Cadence`), `Engine/` (pure `RunwayEngine`, `RunwayProjection`, date-generation sweep — zero SwiftData/SwiftUI imports, compiles and tests standalone), `Persistence/` (App Group constant, `VersionedSchema`, `SchemaMigrationPlan`, shared `ModelContainerFactory`), `Views/Home/`, `Views/Onboarding/`, `Views/List/`, `Views/QuickAdd/`, `Views/Settings/`, `Views/Shared/` (privacy cover, undo toast, empty/error/bills-only states), `Support/` (design tokens as single source of truth). Two test targets: `CisternTests` (engine + persistence), `CisternUITests` (five-home-state screenshot pass, time permitting).

**Data models:** `Bill`/`Paycheck` share shape — `id: UUID`, `amount: Decimal`, `cadence: Cadence`, `anchorDate`, `secondAnchorDate: Date?` (semimonthly's independent second anchor), `createdAt`, `deletedAt: Date?`. `BalanceAnchor` is `amount: Decimal`, `asOf: Date` (freshness and projection start point are measured from `asOf`, not `createdAt`), append-only history. `RunwayProjection` carries `status` (zeroData/billsOnly/fresh/stale), `runwayEndDate`, `daysRemaining`, `fullness`, `freshnessDays`, `narrativeSentence`, and `dailyBalances: [DailyBalancePoint]`.

**Persistence strategy:** `ModelConfiguration` targeting App Group `group.com.cistern.shared`, `cloudKitDatabase: .none`, `VersionedSchema`/`SchemaMigrationPlan` from day one (single version, empty migration stages — shape exists for future use), built via one shared static `ModelContainerFactory.makeContainer(inMemory:)` so a future widget target calls the identical factory. Soft-deletes excluded at the fetch layer via `#Predicate`, never filtered post-fetch in Swift.

**Testing strategy:** `RunwayEngine` suite implements the full named edge-case list from `detailed_discussion`/`ios_architecture_review` as literal `XCTestCase` methods (semimonthly-on-weekend, Feb 29/30/31, biweekly horizon-cap, 31st-clamping for both bills and paychecks, same-day-bias, negative/zero rejection, bills-only honesty), plus new sweep-specific cases (empty bill stream, empty paycheck stream, same-date bill-and-paycheck tie-breaking). Persistence tests use in-memory `ModelConfiguration` and verify the `#Predicate` soft-delete exclusion at the live-fetch level, not just on a manually filtered array.

## Parent Portfolio Prompt

Build 7 completely separate, production-ready iOS apps at the same time.

This is a multi-app PORTFOLIO request: multiple apps in one program. Each selected app becomes its own separate project — one folder per app, never bundled together.

Pick any 7 categories/concepts you like — there is no fixed category assignment. Choose the 7 strongest ideas.

TOP PRIORITY — DESIGN & UI/UX (this outweighs every other consideration):
- Design and UI/UX quality is the single most important success criterion. An app that works but looks generic is a FAILURE here.
- Each app must have a world-class, distinctive visual identity: a deliberate color system, type scale, spacing system, iconography, and motion language — not stock SwiftUI defaults.
- Premium, polished, "Apple Design Award" caliber. Every screen considered: empty states, loading states, error states, transitions.
- Delightful, purposeful microinteractions and animation (haptics, spring transitions, meaningful state changes) — never gratuitous.
- Strong visual hierarchy, generous whitespace, real content density decisions, dark mode, Dynamic Type, and full accessibility (VoiceOver labels, 44pt targets, WCAG AA contrast).
- Each of the 7 apps must look and feel clearly DIFFERENT from the other 6 — seven distinct design directions, not a template recolored.
- Include a documented design system per app (tokens, components, states) and make the built UI actually match it.

SECOND PRIORITY — COMPLEXITY & DEPTH (required):
- Each app must be genuinely COMPLEX and substantial — NOT a simple single-purpose utility or a weekend toy.
- Expect many interconnected features and screens, a real domain data model with relationships, non-trivial business logic and algorithms, meaningful state management, background work, and offline persistence with a clear sync-later architecture.
- Depth that rewards long-term daily use: robust settings, edge-case handling, empty/error/loading states everywhere, data import/export, and at least one genuinely hard technical capability done well.
- Aim for the scope a small team would build over months, not something trivial. Complexity must serve real user value — never complexity for its own sake.

THIRD PRIORITY — MONETIZATION (required):
- Each app must have a clear, sustainable monetization strategy integrated into the product.
- Each app should have a realistic path to revenue: subscription tiers with genuine value-add features, IAP (in-app purchases) for premium features or content, premium versions vs. lite, ad networks (only if user experience respects the user), or a combination.
- Monetization must NOT feel tacked-on or punitive. It must align with the product's core value proposition and user expectations.
- Design the free tier to hook users and deliver real value (so they want to upgrade); design paid tiers to be compelling, not required for basic functionality.
- Estimate realistic pricing (what would users actually pay, what competitors charge, what the market supports).

SPECIAL REQUIREMENTS (must be met):
1. EXACTLY ONE app must be an IDLE GAME (incremental/clicker style). Make it premium-designed and deeply monetized (subscription pass, cosmetics IAP, battle pass, seasonal content).
2. EXACTLY ONE app must be a PUZZLE GAME (strategy, match-3, tetris-style, Sokoban-style, or logic puzzle). Make it engaging, replayable, and deeply monetized (levels as premium content, daily challenges, cosmetics, season pass).
3. The other 5 apps can be ANY category (productivity, utility, social, wellness, creative, finance, education, etc.) — just ensure diversity, complexity, design excellence, and clear monetization.

General Requirements:
1. Each app must go from 0 to production-ready, not just a few features.
2. Each app must be unique, useful, and commercially viable.
3. Each app must provide real end-user value.
4. Each app should have viral potential (broad or niche) without sacrificing usefulness.
5. Each app should be better than its competitors in a meaningful way, especially on design and depth.
6. Each app should be local-first, architected so cloud support can be added later without rewriting.

UNIQUENESS (hard rule, non-negotiable):
- No app in this batch may be similar to another app in this batch.
- No app may be similar to anything already built in this workspace or before (Waylay, TrueScale, Provenance, Scope-Guard, Practice-Loop, Proof, ReturnWise, VerveCoach, CueKeeper, brinekeeper, chalkline, quietpilot, and the ~50+ apps of prior batches).
- Produce differentiated concepts even if a category overlaps.

Bonus points (optional — only when integral and genuinely valuable, never forced): unique use of LLMs, AR, or ML.

Build rules:
- Run the seven app efforts in parallel; keep discussion and design phases separate per app.
- Each app gets its own full product direction, design direction, architecture, implementation plan, and build output.
- Spend real effort in the design, architecture, AND monetization phases — design discussion, design handoff, technical architecture, and monetization strategy must all be thorough and specific.
- Save all phase discussions and final decisions; at the end combine each app's full transcript into a .txt file.
- If an app is liked, also prepare Jira board + Notion project structures for backfilling later.
- Use enough rounds per phase for high-quality discussion, ending early on consensus.
- Recover cleanly from stalls; report only important milestones.

Output:
- One folder per app in the output directory — never one folder containing all apps.
Output directory:
- /Users/pchordia/Documents/iOS-App-Factory

Now generate at least 10 candidate ideas (including both game and non-game concepts), discuss them thoroughly with heavy weight on design potential, depth, AND monetization strategy, choose the best 7 (ensuring exactly 1 idle game and 1 puzzle game), and build all 7 to production quality with exceptional UI/UX, real complexity, and clear monetization paths.

### Round 1


**Codex — Round 1 (skipped: CLI unavailable)**

_Codex skipped — in cooldown (rate_limit)._

**Claude (Backend / Systems Engineer · Skeptic) — Round 1**

Coming at this from the data/systems side, and I want to open by pushing on something that's been quietly inconsistent for two phases now rather than let it ride into the build undetected: `RunwayEngine` is described as "pure, SwiftData-free, SwiftUI-free" and "compiles and tests standalone," but its actual locked signature is `project(bills: [Bill], paychecks: [Paycheck], anchor: BalanceAnchor?, ...)` — and `Bill`, `Paycheck`, `BalanceAnchor` are `@Model` classes, which means the file that declares them imports SwiftData, and anything that takes them as parameters transitively depends on SwiftData whether or not the word "SwiftData" appears in the Engine file itself. That's not pedantry — it means the "Engine has zero SwiftData imports and can be tested without a persistence stack" claim is only true in the narrow sense that it doesn't call `ModelContext` or perform a fetch. In practice, every engine unit test has to construct real `@Model` instances to call `project(...)`, and while that works fine for property-only models with no relationships (which is what we have here, so this isn't a blocker), the project plan needs to say this out loud and pick one of two paths rather than let a builder discover the ambiguity mid-sprint: either (a) accept that the "pure engine" boundary is a testability/isolation property, not a literal zero-SwiftData-import property, and test by constructing bare `@Model` instances outside any container — which works, is simple, and is what I'd actually recommend — or (b) add a genuinely separate plain-struct DTO layer (`BillInput`, `PaycheckInput`) that the engine actually consumes, decoupling it for real. Given this is an MVP and the models have no relationships to complicate free-standing construction, I'd lock (a) now as a plan decision rather than let someone "discover" this contradiction while writing the first engine test and improvise a fix under time pressure.

On sequencing: the tech_specs phase pre-approved a fallback from the sweep-merge algorithm to a boring day-by-day loop "if test coverage is at risk," but a pre-approved escape hatch with no trigger date is exactly the kind of thing that either never gets invoked (because nobody wants to admit they're behind) or gets invoked in a panic the night before the build is due. The plan needs an actual checkpoint: attempt the sweep-merge as part of the data_domain milestone, but set a hard internal checkpoint at the end of that milestone — if the full named edge-case suite (semimonthly-weekend, Feb 29/30/31, horizon-cap, 31st-clamping for both entity types, same-day-bias, negative/zero rejection, bills-only honesty, plus the sweep-specific empty-stream and same-date-tie-break cases) isn't green at that checkpoint, the fallback triggers immediately, not after more time is sunk trying to make the clever version work. I'd rather see the naive loop ship with 100% of that test list passing than the sweep-merge ship with 80% of it passing and a promise to finish later.

More broadly, I think this plan needs to be organized around the four owning_lanes that tech_specs already established — data_domain, services_utilities, primary_ui, polish_resilience — with a strict dependency order: data_domain and services_utilities (models, engine, validation, persistence factory, App Group config) have to be substantially done and tested before primary_ui starts wiring `@Query` against them, because otherwise UI work is guessing at an interface that might still change shape. Polish_resilience (privacy cover, undo toast, empty/error states, accessibility) is explicitly last, not because it's unimportant but because every one of those components wraps or reacts to states the earlier two lanes produce — you can't build a meaningful `DataLoadErrorView` until you know what "ModelContainer construction fails" actually looks like in practice, and you can't build the privacy cover correctly until the sheet-presentation stack it has to sit above actually exists.

I want to flag one concrete, easy-to-skip milestone that has nothing to do with Swift code correctness and everything to do with whether this project builds at all: the App Group entitlement, the literal group identifier in the entitlements file, and target membership/signing configuration have to be real, verified deliverables — not just a string constant referenced in Swift. The pitfalls reference is blunt about this and it's exactly the failure mode I'd expect here: a perfectly correct `ModelContainerFactory.makeContainer()` call will throw at runtime if the `.entitlements` file, provisioning, and bundle identifier don't actually line up, and that failure will masquerade as the "data load failure" edge case (routing to `DataLoadErrorView`) when it's actually a build misconfiguration. I'd make "app launches and successfully creates the App Group–backed ModelContainer on a clean simulator install" an explicit, checked milestone gate before any UI work is trusted, not something assumed to just work because the factory function is written correctly.

Related skepticism about `DataLoadErrorView` itself: its only recovery action is "retry," but for a local on-device SwiftData store, the only real ways `ModelContainer` construction throws are structural (bad entitlement/schema mismatch, disk corruption) — categories where hitting retry again does nothing, because the same misconfiguration is still there. I'm not proposing new scope here, just flagging it plainly in the plan so it doesn't get treated as a fully "solved" edge case: retry mainly covers a genuinely rare disk-level hiccup, and the verification plan should include actually forcing this state (e.g., temporarily pointing the factory at an invalid configuration in a debug harness) rather than assuming the view "must work" because it exists in the file tree.

On the versioned schema and migration plan: I'd explicitly note in the plan that there is nothing to migration-test here, on purpose — one schema version, empty `stages` array, and I don't want a milestone that invents a fake migration scenario just to have "migration test coverage" on a checklist. The value of `VersionedSchema`/`SchemaMigrationPlan` right now is entirely structural (it exists so a real migration later has a shape to slot into), and testing effort should go where it's real: the engine edge cases and the `#Predicate` soft-delete exclusion actually being enforced at the fetch layer, which does need a concrete in-memory-store test, not just code review.

Last thing I'd insist gets carried forward rather than re-derived: the verification plan for this whole app is already fully specified two phases back — the eight-point checklist from `next_steps_small` (2-minute first-run, ≤3-tap add, 1-tap balance update, instant recompute with no spinner, force-quit persistence, no fake $0 empty state, 31st-in-a-30-day-month correctness, past-dated-paycheck handling) plus the named edge-case list from `tech_specs`/`ios_architecture_review`. The project plan's job is to schedule when each of those gets checked and by what mechanism (unit test vs. manual simulator run vs. VoiceOver pass), not to write a new list. One item on that list is inherently not unit-testable and needs to be named as a manual QA step with its own time slot: the VoiceOver focus-returns-then-announcement-fires sequencing is a real runtime race (focus settling is a timing-dependent system behavior), and I don't think it can be meaningfully verified by an XCTest — it needs an actual VoiceOver-enabled simulator or device pass as a scheduled verification task, or it'll quietly get skipped because "the code looks right."

**Coordinator (Claude) — decision after round 1**

Here's where the group landed: same pattern as every phase in this session — Codex still hasn't shown up, so it's one voice again — but this pass earns its keep by turning "build the app" into an actual schedulable plan rather than restating the architecture. Nothing in it reopens or contradicts anything locked in tech_specs or the architecture review; it's the sequencing and verification layer this phase exists to produce.

The key resolution: the "pure engine" claim needed disambiguating before someone hit it mid-sprint. The plan locks the pragmatic reading — `RunwayEngine` is pure in the sense that it never touches `ModelContext`/fetches, but it does take `@Model` instances as parameters, so tests construct bare model instances outside any container rather than building a separate DTO layer. That's a real decision, made now instead of improvised later.

Second resolution: the tech_specs-approved fallback from sweep-merge to a day-by-day loop needed a trigger, not just permission to exist. The plan pins it to a checkpoint — at the end of the data_domain milestone, if the full named edge-case suite isn't green, fall back immediately rather than sinking more time into the clever version.

Third, and this is the most consequential new thing this phase surfaces: the App Group entitlement, literal group ID, and signing/target membership have to be their own verified milestone gate — "app successfully creates the App Group–backed ModelContainer on a clean simulator install" — before any UI work is trusted, because a misconfiguration here would masquerade as the already-designed `DataLoadErrorView` state rather than announcing itself as a build problem.

Everything else is scheduling discipline: strict lane order (data_domain + services_utilities → primary_ui → polish_resilience, since polish wraps states the earlier lanes produce), no invented migration-testing theater for a single-version schema, and the verification plan explicitly reuses the eight-point checklist from `next_steps_small` plus the named edge-case list from `tech_specs`/`ios_architecture_review` rather than rederiving it — with one real addition: the VoiceOver focus-then-announcement sequencing is a runtime race that can't be meaningfully unit-tested and needs its own scheduled manual QA pass.

No disagreement is on the table — there's no second voice to stress-test this against, but it's internally consistent, satisfies both acceptance checks (executable by task assignment; testing/integration scheduled as real milestones, not end-of-project polish), and doesn't drift from anything locked upstream.

## Final Output

**Milestones (in build order):**

1. **Scaffolding & platform gate** — Xcode project/target created, App Group entitlement configured with the literal identifier (`group.com.cistern.shared`), signing/provisioning verified. **Gate: app launches on a clean simulator install and `ModelContainerFactory.makeContainer()` successfully creates an App Group–backed container.** No UI work is trusted before this passes — a misconfigured entitlement fails at runtime in a way that looks identical to the designed `DataLoadErrorView` state, so it must be caught here, not diagnosed later as a "data load failure."

2. **data_domain lane** — `Cadence`, `Bill`, `Paycheck`, `BalanceAnchor` (`@Model`, no relationships), `CisternSchemaV1`/`CisternMigrationPlan` (single version, empty stages — no invented migration test scenario), and `RunwayEngine` including the `occurrences(...)` generator. Engine attempted as the sweep-merge algorithm; tested via bare `@Model` instances constructed outside any container (no separate DTO layer). **Checkpoint at end of this milestone:** run the full named edge-case suite (semimonthly-on-weekend, Feb 29/30/31, horizon-cap, 31st-clamping for both Bill and Paycheck, same-day-bias, negative/zero rejection, bills-only honesty, plus sweep-specific empty-stream and same-date tie-break cases). If not 100% green, fall back to the day-by-day loop immediately — do not carry a partially-tested sweep-merge forward.

3. **services_utilities lane** (parallel with late data_domain) — `validateAmount`/`AmountValidation`, `AppGroupIdentifier`, `ModelContainerFactory`, `requestAccessibilityAnnouncement`. Persistence tests use in-memory `ModelConfiguration` and verify `#Predicate` soft-delete exclusion at the live-fetch level.

4. **primary_ui lane** — `HomeView`, `OnboardingFlow`, `QuickAddSheet`/`QuickAddButton`, `BillsPaychecksListView`, `SettingsView`, `PremiumStubView`. Does not start in earnest until data_domain/services_utilities interfaces are stable, since UI is wired directly against `@Query` and the engine's real output shape.

5. **polish_resilience lane** — `PrivacyCoverView` (root/scene-level), `UndoToast`, `EmptyStateView`, `BillsOnlyBanner`, `DataLoadErrorView`. Deliberately last: these wrap or react to states the earlier lanes produce (you can't build a meaningful data-load-failure screen until you know what that failure actually looks like).

6. **Verification pass** — execute the full checklist below; repair as needed.

7. **Final sign-off** — build verification and review agree the production-readiness bar from the prompt contract is met.

**Dependencies:**
- Milestone 1 blocks everything — it's infrastructure, not Swift correctness, and the plan treats it as a checked gate rather than an assumption.
- data_domain + services_utilities block primary_ui (UI shouldn't guess at an interface that might still change).
- primary_ui blocks polish_resilience (privacy cover needs the real sheet-presentation stack; error/empty states need the real failure/empty shapes).
- The sweep-merge-vs-fallback decision is a hard gate inside data_domain, not a soft aspiration — it resolves before primary_ui starts, so UI is never built against an engine whose final algorithm is still undecided.

**Risk mitigations:**
- *Engine purity ambiguity* → resolved as a documented testing convention (bare `@Model` construction), not a new abstraction layer.
- *Sweep-merge running out of time half-tested* → hard checkpoint with a pre-approved, unconditional fallback trigger, not an open-ended escape hatch.
- *App Group/entitlement misconfiguration masquerading as a data-load bug* → its own explicit, checked milestone gate before UI work begins.
- *`DataLoadErrorView`'s retry action being mostly theater* (most real `ModelContainer` construction failures are structural, not transient) → named explicitly as a limitation; verification plan requires actually forcing the failure state via a debug harness rather than trusting it "must work" because it exists in the file tree.
- *Fake migration-test busywork* → explicitly excluded; testing effort goes to the engine edge cases and `#Predicate` enforcement instead, which are real.
- *VoiceOver focus/announcement race being silently unverified* → called out as not unit-testable, scheduled as its own manual VoiceOver-enabled simulator/device pass rather than assumed correct from code review.

**Verification plan (mechanism-tagged, reusing the locked checklists rather than reinventing them):**
- *Unit test:* full named `RunwayEngine` edge-case suite (per data_domain checkpoint above).
- *Unit test:* in-memory persistence + `#Predicate` soft-delete exclusion at live fetch.
- *Manual simulator run:* App Group container creation on clean install (milestone 1 gate).
- *Manual simulator run:* forced `ModelContainer` construction failure → confirms `DataLoadErrorView` renders and retry behavior is honest about its limits.
- *Manual simulator run:* the eight-point checklist from `next_steps_small` — first-run-to-runway under 2 minutes with no dead-end field, ≤3-tap bill/paycheck add, 1-tap-plus-entry balance update, instant recompute with no spinner, force-quit/relaunch persistence, no fake-precise $0 empty state, 31st-in-a-30-day-month correctness, past-dated-paycheck handling.
- *Manual VoiceOver pass:* sheet-dismiss focus return, then recompute announcement firing after focus settles (not simultaneously) — explicitly not covered by XCTest.
- *Manual pass:* dark mode, Dynamic Type at accessibility sizes (confirms ScrollView reflow, not clipping), 44pt tap targets, WCAG AA contrast per fill-state (fresh/stale/bills-only).

Both acceptance checks are satisfied: the plan is directly executable by a task-assignment phase (each milestone maps to one or more of the four owning_lanes with clear entry/exit conditions), and testing/integration is scheduled throughout — as unit-test checkpoints inside data_domain, as gated milestones for infrastructure, and as explicitly time-slotted manual/VoiceOver passes — not deferred to an undifferentiated "polish" step at the end.

**Quality Gate (Claude) — after round 1**
